Proving a Judge's Prior Ruling: Admission and Testimony
Two methods to prove a judge's prior ruling: admission or testimony, with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two methods of proving a judge's prior ruling and apply the conditions for each, including the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the judge's denial.

Core Idea
A judge's prior ruling is proven by his admission (while in office) or by two witnesses after a valid lawsuit, with a dispute over denial.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains two ways to prove that a judge previously issued a ruling: his own admission or the testimony of two witnesses. Each method has specific conditions: admission only works while the judge is still in office, and testimony requires a valid lawsuit and that the judge does not deny the ruling. However, the Hanafi school has an internal disagreement: Imam Muhammad holds that if the judge denies, the testimony is not accepted, while Abu Hanifa accepts it. The later fatwa prefers Muhammad's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
The passage lists two ways: the judge's own admission (i'tiraf) and the testimony of two witnesses (shahadat). State these clearly.
2
Determine the condition for admission
The judge's admission is accepted only if he is still in office (muttawalliyan). If dismissed, his statement is like that of an ordinary person.
Contrast with post-dismissal: 'ka-wahid min al-ra'aya' – like one of the subjects.
3
Determine the conditions for testimony
Testimony requires a valid lawsuit (da'wa sahiha) against a present opponent, and the judge must not be denying the ruling. If the judge denies, testimony is not accepted according to the preferred view.
Also note the sub-issue: testimony about a ruling made outside the courtroom or city is accepted according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the Sahibayn.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa accepts testimony even if the judge denies; Muhammad ibn al-Hasan rejects it. Identify the thamarah (practical consequence): under Abu Hanifa's view, witnesses can override the judge's denial; under Muhammad's, they cannot.
The dispute is recorded in al-Bazzaziyya and al-Khulasa.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Jami' al-Fusulayn prefers Muhammad's view due to the conditions of judges in later times. Fatwa is given accordingly.
Also noted that Muhammad said 'The judge does not judge by his own knowledge' and that fatwa is by his statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسادس في طريق ثبوته له وجهان أحدهما اعترافه حيث كان متولياً وسيأتي أنه إذا قال قاض عالم عدل قضيت على هذا بالقطع أو بالقتل وسعك فعله وإن لم تعاين سببه، وأما إذا كان معزولاً فهو كواحد من الرعايا لا يقبل قوله مطلقاً إلا فيما إذا كان في يده كما سيأتي. وفي السراج الوهاج : الحاكم إذا حكم بحق ثم قال بعد عزله كنت حكمت لفلان بكذا لم يقبل قوله اهـ. الثاني أن يشهد شاهد إن على حكمه بعد دعوى صحيحة إن لم يكن القاضي . منكراً. قال في البزازية والخلاصة وإن أرادوا أن يثبتوا حكم الخليفة عند الأصل فلا بد من تقديم دعوى صحيحة على خصم حاضر وإقامة البيئة كما لو أردوا إثبات قضاء قاض آخر اهـ. وفي البزازية أيضاً : شهدا على القاضي أنه قضى في غير مجلس القضاء أو خارج المصر تقبل عنده خلافاً لهما .اهـ. قيدنا بعدم إنكاره لأنهما لو شهدا أنه قضى بكذا وقال لم أقض بشيء لا تقبل شهادتهما خلافاً لمحمد ; كذا في البزازية .اهـ. ورجح في جامع الفصولين قول محمد قال : وينبغي أن يفتى به لما علم من أحوال قضاة زماننا ثم نقل أن محمداً قال : لا يقضي القاضي بعلمه . ثم نقل عن عيون المذاهب أن بقوله يفتى.
And the sixth [section] on the way of its proof: there are two aspects. One: his admission when he was in office. It will come that if he says, 'I am a knowledgeable, just judge; I gave judgment on this one by amputation or by death, and it is permissible for you to act upon it even if you do not witness its cause.' But if he is dismissed, he is like one of the subjects; his statement is not accepted absolutely except what is in his hand, as will come. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: The ruler if he judged with truth then said after his dismissal, 'I judged for so-and-so with such-and-such,' his statement is not accepted. Second: that two witnesses testify to his ruling after a valid lawsuit, provided the judge is not denying. He said in al-Bazzaziyya and al-Khulasa: If they want to prove the ruling of the caliph at the original capital, it is necessary to present a valid lawsuit against a present opponent and establish evidence, just as if they wanted to prove the judgment of another judge. In al-Bazzaziyya also: Two witnesses testified that the judge ruled in a non-courtroom or outside the city; it is accepted according to him (Abu Hanifa), contrary to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). We restricted it to his not denying, because if they testified that he ruled with such-and-such and he said, 'I did not rule anything,' their testimony is not accepted, contrary to Muhammad. Thus in al-Bazzaziyya. And it is strengthened in Jami' al-Fusulayn by the statement of Muhammad; he said: 'It is appropriate to give fatwa by it due to the known conditions of the judges of our time.' Then he transmitted that Muhammad said: 'The judge does not judge by his own knowledge.' Then he transmitted from 'Uyun al-Madhahib that by his statement (Muhammad's) fatwa is given.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on how to prove a judge's prior judgment. It is the sixth section of the discussion, describing two methods. First, the judge's own admission while he was still in office. If a current judge says he previously ruled on a case, his word is acceptable even if the reason is not visible, as long as he was a qualified judge at the time. However, if he is dismissed, his claim is like that of an ordinary person and is not accepted, except for matters within his physical possession. The commentary cites al-Siraj al-Wahhaj to reinforce that a judge's post-dismissal claim about a past ruling is rejected. Second, the testimony of two witnesses. They must testify after a valid lawsuit (dawa sahiha) has been filed against a present opponent, and the judge must not be denying the ruling. If the judge denies, the testimony is not accepted according to Abu Hanifa, but Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani holds that it is accepted. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq mentions that the view of Muhammad is preferred in Jami' al-Fusulayn and that fatwa is given accordingly, given the conditions of judges in later times. Additionally, the passage notes that if two witnesses testify that the judge ruled outside the courtroom or outside the city, it is accepted according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). However, if the judge denies, even the two disciples' view is rejected? Actually it's a nuanced ikhtilaf. The passage also mentions that to prove the ruling of a caliph (or high ruler), the procedure is the same as for any other judge: a valid lawsuit and evidence are required.

Key Terms
اعترافه (i'tirafuhu): The judge's own admission that he gave a ruling.
دعوى صحيحة (da'wa sahiha): A valid legal lawsuit meeting all conditions (e.g., against a present opponent, clear subject).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
To prove a judge's prior ruling, his admission is accepted only while in office; testimony requires a valid lawsuit and is rejected if the judge denies (preferred view).

Finality of Judgment and Retraction: The Hanafi Stance on Judicial Error
Seventh section on rulings of a judge, focusing on finality and retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle of finality of a judge's judgment in mujtahad fih matters and identify the conditions under which a judge may retract, distinguishing the consequences for intentional vs. erroneous judgments.

Core Idea
A judge's valid judgment is final; retraction is only allowed if error is proven by evidence or the winner's admission.

Imagine that after a judge delivers a verdict, new evidence surfaces showing the witnesses were not qualified. Can the judge change the ruling himself? On whose authority? And what if the judge acted deliberately? This lesson addresses these questions directly from the Hanafi fiqh of qada, revealing the delicate balance between judicial authority and accountability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the First Principle – Finality
Locate the statement that a judgment in a matter of ijtihad, when all legal conditions are met, cannot be nullified by anyone. This is the rule of luzum (binding force).
The source uses the term 'لزوم' to indicate the judgment's immunity from being overturned.
2
Find When Retraction is Permissible
Examine the conditions under which the judge himself may retract. The source mentions that if a clear text (nass) appears contradicting the earlier judgment, he must nullify it. Also, he may retract if the original judgment was erroneous.
Note the distinction: retraction is not a right in every case; it is limited to error.
3
Distinguish Intentional vs. Erroneous Error
Read the consequences of the judge's state of mind. If he intentionally acted wrongly, he pays damages from his own wealth and receives discretionary punishment. If he erred, the blood money is paid from the public treasury (bayt al-mal).
This distinction protects the judge from personal liability in cases of honest mistake.
4
Separate Rights of People (Huquq al-‘Ibad) from Rights of Allah (Huquq Allah)
Observe how the rule differs for matters like divorce, emancipation, and qisas (rights of people) versus hudud crimes (rights of Allah). Both categories follow the same principle, but the source notes different remedies (e.g., return of woman to husband in divorce vs. compensation for hudud).
The source details specific outcomes: the woman is returned, the slave is restored, etc.
5
Analyze Modes of Proving Error
Learn what evidence establishes that a judgment was erroneous. The source states: error is proven by external evidence (bayyina) or by the admission of the winning party (iqrar al-muqada lahu). The judge's own admission of error does NOT suffice—similar to a witness withdrawing testimony.
This prevents misuse: a judge cannot unilaterally overturn a judgment based on his own claim of error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع في أحكامه فمنها بالنسبة إلى الحكم اللزوم فليس لأحد نقضه حيث كان مجتهداً فيه ومستوفياً شرائطه الشرعية وهل يصح رجوع القاضي رجع ورده، وإن كان مختلفاً الخلاصة والبزازية : للقاضي أن يرجع عن قضائه إن كان خطأ عنه؟ ففي فيه أمضاه وقضى فيما يأتي بما هو عنده فإن ظهر له نص بخلاف قضائه نقضه، ثم إن كان في حقوق العباد كالطلاق والعتاق والقصاص أو ظهر أن الشهود عبيد أو محدودون في قذف إن قال القاضي تعمدت فالضمان في ماله ويعزر للجناية وإن أخطأ يضمن الدية، وفي الطلاق والعتاق ترد المرأة إلى الزوج والرقيق إلى المولى، وفي حقوقه تعالى كالزنا والشرب إذا حد وبان الشهود عبيداً وقال تعمدت الحكم يضمن في ماله الدية، وفي الخطأ يضمن من مزيد تقرير فيه فراجع كلاً من المحلين وتأمل قوله : وبه) علم أن الاتصالات والتنافيذ الخ) قال الرملي : وسيذكره أيضاً في شرح قوله وإذا رفع إليه حكم حاكم أمضاه» اهـ. أي في باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي :قوله للقاضي أن يرجع عن قضائه الخ قال الرملي : وفي مسائل شتى آخر المتن إذا قضى القاضي في حادثة ببينة ثم قال رجعت عن قضائي أو بدا لي غير ذلك أو وقفت على  كتاب القضاء ٤٣٥ عن السلطان حتى هـ. من بيت المال. هذا إذا ظهر الخطأ بالبينة أو بإقرار المقضى له، أما إذا أقر القاضي بذلك لا يثبت الخطأ كما لو الشاهد عن الشهادة لا يبطل القضاء ا هـ . وإذا أقر المقضى له ببطلانه بطل رجع إلا المقضي بحريته كما في البزازية وبالنسبة إلى التولية عدمه
The seventh [section] on its rulings, including with respect to the ruling: finality, so no one may nullify it when it is in a matter of ijtihad and its legal conditions are fulfilled. And is it valid for the judge to retract? He retracts and rejects. Even if there is difference, al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya: The judge may retract his judgment if it was error. He said: In it, he executes it and judges in the future according to what he has. If a text appears contrary to his judgment, he nullifies it. Then, if it is in rights of people like divorce, emancipation, and qisas, or if it appears that the witnesses were slaves or had been flogged for slander, if the judge says, 'I intentionally acted,' then the guarantee (damages) is from his own wealth and he is given discretionary punishment for the crime; if he erred, he guarantees the blood money; in divorce and emancipation, the woman is returned to the husband and the slave to the master. In the rights of Allah like zina and drinking, if he had carried out the hadd and it appears the witnesses were slaves and he says, 'I intentionally judged,' he guarantees the blood money from his wealth; in error, he guarantees from... more detail is there, so refer to both places and contemplate his statement: 'And by it (the connection and endorsements etc.)' Al-Ramli said: He will also mention it in the commentary of his statement: 'And if there is brought to him the judgment of a judge, he executes it.' That is in the chapter of the judge's letter to another judge. His statement: 'The judge may retract his judgment etc.' Al-Ramli said: In various issues at the end of the text, if the judge judged in an incident with evidence then said, 'I retract my judgment,' or 'otherwise occurred to me,' or 'I stopped at' [PAGE_435] the ruler until... from the treasury. This is if the error appears by evidence or by the admission of the one in whose favor judgment was given. As for if the judge admits that [error], the error is not established, just as if the witness withdraws from his testimony, the judgment is not invalidated. And if the one in whose favor judgment was given admits its invalidity, it becomes invalid and he returns, except the one declared free, as in al-Bazzaziyya. And with respect to appointment (tawliya), it is not [final]?
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is the seventh section of the chapter on judgeship, covering the effects and rulings of a judge's judgment. It first establishes the principle of finality (luzum): a judgment that is based on ijtihad and fulfills all legal conditions cannot be overturned by anyone. However, the judge himself may retract (yarji') if the judgment was erroneous. The commentary explains that if a judge discovers a clear text (nass) contradicting his previous judgment, he must reverse it and decide accordingly in the future. The passage then details the consequences of error or intentional wrongdoing. In cases involving the rights of people (huquq al-ibad) such as divorce, emancipation, and qisas, or if witnesses are later found to be disqualified, if the judge admits intentional wrongdoing, he must pay damages from his own wealth and receive discretionary punishment; if he erred, he pays blood money (diya). In cases involving rights of Allah (hudud) like zina and drinking, similar rules apply: intentional wrongdoing leads to personal liability, while error requires payment from the public treasury (bayt al-mal). The text also discusses retraction: if the error is proven by evidence or the winning party admits it, the judgment is reversed; but if the judge alone admits error, his admission does not establish error (unlike a witness's retraction). A special exception: if a person was declared free, that judgment stands even if the winning party later admits a mistake. The passage ends with a note that regarding appointment to office (tawliya), the same principle of finality does not apply (the ruler can dismiss the judge).

Key Terms
لزوم (luzum): Finality, binding effect; the judgment cannot be overturned by anyone.
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction by the judge of his own judgment.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Muslims, from which compensation is paid for judicial errors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a judge intentionally gives a wrong judgment in a case of qisas (retaliation)?
A) The judgment stands and the judge is not liable.
B) The judge pays damages from his own wealth and is punished.
C) The judge pays from the public treasury.
D) The case is retried by a different judge.

Key Takeaway
A valid judgment in a matter of ijtihad is final; retraction is allowed only when error is proven by concrete evidence or the winning party's admission, not by the judge's own claim, with clear financial consequences for intentional vs. mistaken error.

Ruler's Power to Dismiss a Judge
Ruling on the ruler's dismissal power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the ruler's dismissal of a judge and identify the parallel authority of the chief judge over deputies.

Core Idea
The ruler may dismiss a judge for any reason, even without suspicion—tenure is discretionary.

Judicial independence is a key principle, but classical Hanafi fiqh grants the ruler broad discretionary power to dismiss a judge. This lesson examines the ruling from al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya that dismissal is valid for suspicion or even without cause, and extends to the chief judge's authority over his deputies. Understanding this power clarifies the relationship between political authority and the judiciary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cited authorities
The passage begins by citing two major Hanafi fatwa collections: al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya. Recognize these as reliable sources of later Hanafi rulings (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
These works represent the established position in the school.
2
State the ruling on dismissal
The ruler may dismiss a judge for suspicion (riba) or even without suspicion. The word 'riba' here refers to doubt about integrity, but the ruling is broader.
The Hanafi school does not require a specific cause.
3
Apply to the chief judge
The author adds that the chief judge (qadi al-qudat) also has the authority to dismiss his deputy for a misdeed or otherwise.
This extends the same discretionary power to delegated judicial authorities.
4
Note the recommendation on tenure
Later in the passage, it is mentioned that a judge should not remain in office more than a year to avoid forgetting knowledge. This is a recommendation (sunnah), not a binding rule.
This is a guideline for the ruler's discretion.

And in al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya: The ruler may dismiss the judge for suspicion or for no suspicion. I said: The chief judge may dismiss his deputy for a misdeed or otherwise. Among them [these rulings]: that if judgeship is delegated to two, neither may exercise judicial authority alone; but if they stipulate that each acts independently, there is no transmitted text on it, and Imam Zahir al-Din said: It should be permissible, because the deputy judge is a deputy who is not dismissed by the dismissal of the judge and possesses independence. When a month passes and she has borrowed and the evidence is upright, she takes her maintenance from the time he prescribed for her. Among them: he cannot appoint a deputy except with explicit permission or by indication, such as saying 'I made you chief judge.' Among them: the judge should not remain more than a year so that he does not forget knowledge. Among them: he restricts [his judgment] to the person judged against and to all who receive the property from him, and does not extend to the general public; but it extends in judgments regarding freedom, lineage, allegiance, and marriage; but does not extend in awqaf according to the most correct view, and we presented it in the chapter of entitlement from sales. The eighth on what removes the judge from judgeship: in al-Bazzaziyya: Four traits: [including] deceiving witnesses, 'I nullified my judgment,' and the like are not considered, and the judgment is executed if it was after a valid lawsuit and upright testimony. Ibn Wahban said, and it is understood from the restriction that if he judged by his own knowledge, he may retract, as if someone confessed a right to him then both left, then two came and disputed before him, and he judged for one thinking he was the confessor, then it turned out he was someone else; then he should not execute his judgment. It is supported by what is in al-Qunyah from Abu Hamid: He judged in an incident then his error became apparent to him; he must annul his judgment. He said: This is contrary to if he judged in a matter of ijtihad and then saw a different view; he may not retract nor may others annul it as long as it does not contradict the Book, Sunnah, or consensus. His statement: 'And with respect to appointment, it does not (have finality)' is connected to his statement 'with respect to the judgment,' and the pronoun in 'it does not' refers to finality. His statement: 'or the religion' will soon come from al-Walwalijiyya something that contradicts it, with a reconciliation between them. His statement: 'It is appropriate that the opponent if he knew...'

Commentary
This passage covers numerous rulings on the appointment and dismissal of judges and related matters. It begins with the ruler's authority to dismiss a judge for any reason, even without suspicion. The chief judge (qadi al-qudat) may similarly dismiss his deputy for misconduct or otherwise. If judgeship is delegated to two persons, generally neither may act alone, but if they stipulate independence, it is permissible according to Imam Zahir al-Din. The appointment can be suspended (ta'liq) or restricted to a specific time or place; if no place is specified, the judge has authority only over his own city. The passage also discusses a case about maintenance for a woman claiming marriage: the judge may provisionally prescribe maintenance with a conditional statement. Other rulings include: the judgment of an unauthorized person (fuduli) becomes valid if the proper judge endorses it; a judge with limited hours may only judge during his appointed times unless endorsed; if he excludes certain cases, they are not to be judged; he cannot appoint a deputy without explicit permission; he should not remain in office more than a year to avoid forgetting knowledge; his judgment only affects the specific parties in property cases but extends to everyone in personal status matters (freedom, lineage, etc.). The eighth section discusses what removes a judge from office: four traits, including deceiving witnesses and declaring his judgment void, are ineffective if the judgment was valid. Finally, the text addresses when a judge may retract a judgment based on his own knowledge (if he realizes error) versus when he may not (if it is a matter of ijtihad and no clear text contradicts). The passage ends with a note about a further discussion on 'or the religion' from al-Walwalijiyya. Despite fragmentation, the core rulings are identifiable.

Key Terms
ريبة (riba): Suspicion or doubt about the judge's integrity.
فضولي (fuduli): An unauthorized person who claims to act as a judge without appointment.
استخلاف (istikhlaf): Appointing a deputy or successor.

Key Takeaway

Suspension and Scope of Judicial Authority
Rulings on restrictions and scope of judicial authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rules on suspending judgeship by time or place, and apply the scope of a judgment's effects in property vs. personal status cases.

Core Idea
A judge's authority can be restricted by time or place; property judgments bind only parties, but personal status judgments bind all.

The appointment of a judge is not necessarily open-ended; the ruler may suspend, add conditions, or restrict the judge's jurisdiction to a specific city. Moreover, the effects of a judgment differ depending on the subject matter. This lesson unpacks these nuanced rules from the passage, showing how Hanafi fiqh balances judicial authority with practical limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the forms of restriction
The passage states that judgeship can be suspended (ta'liq), added to, or restricted to a time or place. If no place is specified, the chosen view is that the judge becomes a judge only in his own city, not the entire realm.
This parallels the principle of ikhtisas in appointments.
2
Examine the case of deputy appointment
A judge cannot appoint a deputy without explicit permission or a clear indication (e.g., being called 'chief judge'). This limits delegation.
Permission must be explicit or by clear implication.
3
Differentiate scope of judgments
In property disputes, the judgment only binds the specific parties and those who take property from them. In personal status matters (freedom, lineage, allegiance, marriage), the judgment extends to all. In awqaf (endowments), it does not extend according to the most correct view.
This distinction is based on the nature of the right: property rights are relative, personal status rights are absolute.
4
Apply to the maintenance case
The passage gives an example: a woman claiming marriage presents evidence. The judge may provisionally prescribe maintenance with a conditional statement ('if you are his wife...'). This illustrates that a judge can award temporary maintenance even before final proof.
The judgment is conditional and becomes final only if the evidence is later upheld.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخلاصة والبزازية : للسلطان أن يعزل القاضي لريبة أو لغير ريبة ا قلت : ولقاضي القضاة عزل نائبه بجنحة وغيرها، ومنها أن القضاء إذا فوض لاثنين لا يلي القضاء أحدهما فلو شرط أن ينفرد كل منهما بالقضاء لا رواية فيه وقال الإمام ظهير الدين: ينبغي أن يجوز لأن نائب القاضي نائب لا ينعزل بانعزال القاضي ويملك التفرد ; كذا في البزازية. ومنها صحة تعليقه وإضافته وتقييده بزمان ومكان ولو لم يقيده ببلد فالمختار أنه . يصير قاضياً ببلده الذي هو فيه لا في كل بلاد السلطان وهذا في تعليق الولاية وهل يصح تعليق ولاية القضاء؟ قال في نفقات خزانة المفتين : امرأة أقامت على رجل بينة بالنكاح فلا نفقة لها في مدة المسألة عن الشهود ولو أراد القاضي أن يفرض لها النفقة لما رأى المصلحة أن يقول لها ينبغي إن كنت امرأته قد فرضت لك عليه في كل شهر كذا ويشهد على ذلك، فإذا مضى شهر وقد استدانت وعدلت البينة أخذت نفقتها منذ فرض لها اهـ. وعلى هذا فقول القاضي حكمت بكذا إن لم يمنع مانع شرعي صحيح. ومن أحكامه أنه لو قضى فضولي فأجاز القاضي قضاءه جاز، ولو كان مولى في كل أسبوع يومين فقضى في غير اليومين توقف قضاؤه، فإن أجازه في نوبته جاز كما في آخر جامع الفصولين ; كذا في البزازية. ولو استثنى حوادث فلان لا يقضي فيها ولو قضى لا ينفذ. ومنها أنها لا يملك الاستخلاف إلا بإذن صريح أو دلالة بأن يقول له جعلتك قاضي القضاة ومنها أن القاضي لا يبقى أكثر من سنة كي لا ينسى العلم. ومنها أنه يقتصر على المقضى عليه وعلى كل من تلقى الملك منه ولا يتعدى إلى الكافة ويتعدى في القضاء بالحرية والنسب والولاء والنكاح، ولا يتعدى في الوقف على الأصح وقدمناه في باب الاستحقاق من البيوع الثامن فيما يخرج القاضي عن القضاء ففي البزازية : أربع خصال تلبيس الشهود وأبطلت حكمي ونحو ذلك لا يعتبر والقضاء ماض إن كان بعد دعوى صحيحة و شهادة مستقيمة . قال ابن وهبان ويفهم التقييد أنه إذا كان قضى بعلمه يجوز له الرجوع كأن يعترف عنده الآخر بحق ثم غابا ثم جاء اثنان تداعيا عنده فحكم لأحدهما ظاناً أنه المعترف ثم تبين أنه غيره فإنه ينبغي له أن لا يمضي حكمه. ويؤيده ما في القنية عن أبي حامد قضى في حادثة ثم ظهر له خطؤه يجب عليه أن ينقض قضاءه اهـ . قال : وهذا بخلاف ما إذا قضى في مجتهد فيه رأى خلافه ليس له أن يرجع عن حكمه ولا لغيره أن ينقضه ما لم يخالف الكتاب أو السنة أو الإجماع. قوله : (وبالنسبة إلى التولية عدمه معطوف عليه قوله بالنسبة إلى الحكم والضمير في «عدمه» للزوم. قوله : (أو الدين) سيأتي قريباً عن الولوالجية ما يخالفه مع الجمع بينهما قوله : (وينبغي أن الخصم لو علم
And in al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya: The ruler may dismiss the judge for suspicion or for no suspicion. I said: The chief judge may dismiss his deputy for a misdeed or otherwise. Among them [these rulings]: that if judgeship is delegated to two, neither may exercise judicial authority alone; but if they stipulate that each acts independently, there is no transmitted text on it, and Imam Zahir al-Din said: It should be permissible, because the deputy judge is a deputy who is not dismissed by the dismissal of the judge and possesses independence. Thus in al-Bazzaziyya. Among them: the validity of suspending, adding, and restricting it to a time or place. If he did not restrict it to a country, the chosen view is that he becomes a judge in his own city, not in all the ruler's lands. This is in suspending appointment. Is it valid to suspend the office of judgeship? He said in Nafaqat Khizanat al-Muftin: A woman presented evidence of marriage against a man; she has no maintenance during the period of questioning the witnesses. If the judge wants to grant her maintenance, when he sees it beneficial, he says to her: 'If you are his wife, I have prescribed for you upon him so much each month,' and he witnesses that. When a month passes and she has borrowed and the evidence is upright, she takes her maintenance from the time he prescribed for her. Upon this, the judge's statement 'I have judged such-and-such' is valid provided there is no legal impediment. Among its rulings: if an unauthorized person (fuduli) judged and the judge validates his judgment, it is valid. If one is appointed for two days each week and he judged on other days, his judgment is suspended; if the judge validates it on his appointed time, it is valid, as in the end of Jami' al-Fusulayn; thus in al-Bazzaziyya. If he excludes incidents of so-and-so, he may not judge in them; if he judges, it is not executed. Among them: he cannot appoint a deputy except with explicit permission or by indication, such as saying 'I made you chief judge.' Among them: the judge should not remain more than a year so that he does not forget knowledge. Among them: he restricts [his judgment] to the person judged against and to all who receive the property from him, and does not extend to the general public; but it extends in judgments regarding freedom, lineage, allegiance, and marriage; but does not extend in awqaf according to the most correct view, and we presented it in the chapter of entitlement from sales. The eighth on what removes the judge from judgeship: in al-Bazzaziyya: Four traits: [including] deceiving witnesses, 'I nullified my judgment,' and the like are not considered, and the judgment is executed if it was after a valid lawsuit and upright testimony. Ibn Wahban said, and it is understood from the restriction that if he judged by his own knowledge, he may retract, as if someone confessed a right to him then both left, then two came and disputed before him, and he judged for one thinking he was the confessor, then it turned out he was someone else; then he should not execute his judgment. It is supported by what is in al-Qunyah from Abu Hamid: He judged in an incident then his error became apparent to him; he must annul his judgment. He said: This is contrary to if he judged in a matter of ijtihad and then saw a different view; he may not retract nor may others annul it as long as it does not contradict the Book, Sunnah, or consensus. His statement: 'And with respect to appointment, it does not (have finality)' is connected to his statement 'with respect to the judgment,' and the pronoun in 'it does not' refers to finality. His statement: 'or the religion' will soon come from al-Walwalijiyya something that contradicts it, with a reconciliation between them. His statement: 'It is appropriate that the opponent if he knew...'
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage covers numerous rulings on the appointment and dismissal of judges and related matters. It begins with the ruler's authority to dismiss a judge for any reason, even without suspicion. The chief judge (qadi al-qudat) may similarly dismiss his deputy for misconduct or otherwise. If judgeship is delegated to two persons, generally neither may act alone, but if they stipulate independence, it is permissible according to Imam Zahir al-Din. The appointment can be suspended (ta'liq) or restricted to a specific time or place; if no place is specified, the judge has authority only over his own city. The passage also discusses a case about maintenance for a woman claiming marriage: the judge may provisionally prescribe maintenance with a conditional statement. Other rulings include: the judgment of an unauthorized person (fuduli) becomes valid if the proper judge endorses it; a judge with limited hours may only judge during his appointed times unless endorsed; if he excludes certain cases, they are not to be judged; he cannot appoint a deputy without explicit permission; he should not remain in office more than a year to avoid forgetting knowledge; his judgment only affects the specific parties in property cases but extends to everyone in personal status matters (freedom, lineage, etc.). The eighth section discusses what removes a judge from office: four traits, including deceiving witnesses and declaring his judgment void, are ineffective if the judgment was valid. Finally, the text addresses when a judge may retract a judgment based on his own knowledge (if he realizes error) versus when he may not (if it is a matter of ijtihad and no clear text contradicts). The passage ends with a note about a further discussion on 'or the religion' from al-Walwalijiyya. Despite fragmentation, the core rulings are identifiable.

Key Terms
ريبة (riba): Suspicion or doubt about the judge's integrity.
فضولي (fuduli): An unauthorized person who claims to act as a judge without appointment.
استخلاف (istikhlaf): Appointing a deputy or successor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen Hanafi view, if a judge is appointed without specifying a territorial limit, where does his authority apply?
A) Throughout the entire territory of the ruler.
B) Only in the city where the judge resides at the time of appointment.
C) Only in the city where the ruler has his capital.
D) The judge may choose any city he wishes.

Key Takeaway
A judge's authority can be restricted by time or place; if unspecified, it is limited to his own city; and judgments in property are relative, while personal status judgments are universal.

Validity and Retraction of Judgments
Rulings on retraction and validity of judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on when a judge may retract a judgment, distinguish between error of fact and ijtihad, and explain the validity of judgments by unauthorized persons.

Core Idea
A judge may retract only if he discovers an error of fact; a difference of ijtihad does not justify retraction.

Not all judicial mistakes are treated equally. The passage clarifies that if a judge realizes a factual error—like mistaking the identity of a party—he must annul the judgment. But if the case involves ijtihad (interpretation) and he later adopts a different view, he cannot retract. This lesson also covers the validity of judgments by unauthorized persons (fuduli) upon endorsement, and the effect of a judge's statement 'I nullify my judgment' when the original judgment was valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule on fuduli judgments
If an unauthorized person (fuduli) judges and the proper judge later validates that judgment, it becomes valid. This is a form of subsequent ratification.
The validation must come from the authorized judge.
2
Apply the 'exclusion of incidents' rule
If the ruler instructs the judge to exclude certain types of cases (e.g., 'do not hear cases involving so-and-so'), any judgment given in those cases is not executed.
The judge must follow such restrictions.
3
Distinguish between error of fact and ijtihad
The passage gives an example: a judge hears two disputants and judges for one thinking he was the confessor, then realizes his mistake. He should not execute the judgment and must annul it. In contrast, if he ruled on a matter of ijtihad (e.g., interpreting a contract) and later changes his opinion, he cannot retract.
The difference is between factual error (mistake in reality) and interpretive change (difference of opinion).
4
Note the exception for clear textual violation
If the judgment contradicts the Qur'an, Sunnah, or consensus, it may be annulled even if it was an ijtihad error. This is a limit on the finality of ijtihad-based judgments.
The passage references 'unless it contradicts the Book, Sunnah, or consensus.'

Error in Judgment: Evidence vs. Judge's Admission
Ruling on error discovered by evidence or confession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of a judge discovering an error in his judgment, and distinguish between error proven by evidence (bayyinah), confession of the losing party, and the judge's own admission.

Core Idea
A judge's own admission of error does not invalidate his judgment; only evidence or the losing party's confession does.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi judicial procedure. When a judge discovers an error after issuing a verdict, the validity of that verdict depends on how the error comes to light. The author explains that the judge's own realization or admission is insufficient to overturn the judgment, analogizing it to a witness recanting testimony. Instead, only external evidence (bayyinah) or a confession by the losing party can render the judgment void. An exception is noted for cases involving emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
Read the first sentence: 'هذا إذا ظهر الخطأ بالبينة أو بإقرار المقضى له' – the error must appear by bayyinah or by the confession of the one against whom judgment was given. This establishes the general principle.
The term 'المقضى له' is ambiguous; from context, it means the losing party (maqdi alayh).
2
Contrast the judge's own admission
The text then says 'أما إذا أقر القاضي بذلك لا يثبت الخطأ' – if the judge confesses, the error is not established. Note the analogy to a witness recanting: the original judgment stands.
The judge's admission is considered weak like a recanting witness.
3
Apply the exception for confession of the losing party
The text states: 'وإذا أقر المقضى له ببطلانه بطل رجع إلا المقضي بحريته' – if the losing party confesses the judgment is void, it becomes void and he can retract, except in freedom cases. Understand why freedom is an exception: once proven, it's irreversible.
The exception underlines the sanctity of freedom in Islamic law.
4
Consider the corollary for appointments
The passage ends with 'وبالنسبة إلى التولية عدمه' – regarding appointments, the judge's admission does not invalidate. This extends the same rule to administrative appointments.
Appointments (tawliyah) are treated like judgments; the appointee's position remains valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا إذا ظهر الخطأ بالبينة أو بإقرار المقضى له، أما إذا أقر القاضي بذلك لا يثبت الخطأ كما لو الشاهد عن الشهادة لا يبطل القضاء ا هـ . وإذا أقر المقضى له ببطلانه بطل رجع إلا المقضي بحريته كما في البزازية وبالنسبة إلى التولية عدمه
This is when the error appears by evidence or by the confession of the one against whom judgment was given. However, if the judge confesses to that, the error is not established, just as if the witness recants his testimony, the judgment is not invalidated. And if the one against whom judgment was given confesses its invalidity, it becomes invalid and he can retract, except regarding a judgment of freedom (i.e., emancipation), as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And regarding appointment (to a position), it is not (invalid).
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the effect of discovering an error after a judgment has been issued. The text distinguishes between how the error is proven. If the error is established by clear evidence (bayyinah) or by the confession of the losing party (maqdi alayh), then the judgment is invalidated and the matter is retried. However, if the judge himself admits his error, that does not invalidate the judgment, analogously to a witness recanting his testimony—the original judgment stands. This is based on the principle that the judge's own admission may be due to misgiving or forgetfulness, and the judgment was issued based on the evidence presented. An exception is made when the losing party confesses the judgment is void: then it becomes void and he can reclaim what was taken, except in cases of freedom (such as a slave declared free), because once freedom is proven it cannot be reversed. The text also notes that regarding appointments (tawliyah), the judge's admission of error does not invalidate the appointment.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Clear evidence, typically two witnesses or conclusive proof, as opposed to mere confession or judicial notice.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Confession or admission by a party before the court, which can invalidate a judgment if made by the losing party.
القضاء (al-qada'): The judge's formal verdict binding on the parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the judge himself admits that he made an error in his judgment?
A) The judgment is automatically invalidated and must be retried.
B) The error is not established and the judgment remains valid.
C) The judgment becomes void only if the winning party agrees.
D) The judge must pay compensation from his own money.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi judgment stands even if the judge later believes he erred; only external evidence or the opposing party's confession can overturn it.

Sultan's Power to Dismiss Judge and Delegation to Two Judges
Rulings on dismissal and delegation of judicial authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the sultan's power to dismiss a judge and the conditions for valid delegation of judicial authority to two judges.

Core Idea
The sultan may dismiss a judge without cause, but delegation to two judges requires joint action unless stipulated otherwise.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that collects several rulings from the Hanafi sources al-Khulasah and al-Bazzaziyyah regarding the judicial hierarchy. The author discusses the sultan's authority to dismiss a judge, the chief judge's power over his deputy, and the default rule when judicial authority is delegated to two persons. An unresolved question about independent action is addressed with a reasoned opinion from Imam Zahir al-Din, illustrating how later authorities resolve gaps in the transmitted corpus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on the sultan's dismissal authority
The text states the sultan may dismiss a judge 'for suspicion or without suspicion.' Note the sweeping nature of this power under Hanafi law.
This is from later sources (fatawa mutakhkhirin), not the classical Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the chief judge's power to dismiss his deputy
The chief judge (qadi al-qudat) may dismiss his deputy for an offense or otherwise, showing a similar breadth of authority within the hierarchy.
The deputy (na'ib) is not automatically dismissed when the appointing judge is dismissed.
3
Understand the default rule for delegation to two judges
When judicial authority is delegated to two persons, they must act jointly; one alone may not assume the judiciary.
This prevents conflict of judgments.
4
Analyse the permissive view on independent action
If the delegator stipulates that each may act independently, there is no transmitted ruling. Imam Zahir al-Din argues it should be permissible because the deputy is not dismissed by the dismissal of the appointing judge and can possess independence.
This shows how later scholars use analogy and principles to fill gaps.
5
Note the authority level of the sources
The rulings are from al-Khulasah and al-Bazzaziyyah, which are later compilations (fatawa mutakhkhirin). The mufta bihi position follows these sources.
Understand the hierarchy: Zahir al-Riwayah > Nawadir > Fatawa Mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخلاصة والبزازية : للسلطان أن يعزل القاضي لريبة أو لغير ريبة ا قلت : ولقاضي القضاة عزل نائبه بجنحة وغيرها، ومنها أن القضاء إذا فوض لاثنين لا يلي القضاء أحدهما فلو شرط أن ينفرد كل منهما بالقضاء لا رواية فيه وقال الإمام ظهير الدين: ينبغي أن يجوز لأن نائب القاضي نائب لا ينعزل بانعزال القاضي ويملك التفرد؛ كذا في البزازية.
And in al-Khulasah and al-Bazzaziyyah: The sultan may dismiss the judge for suspicion or without suspicion. I say: And the chief judge (qadi al-qudat) may dismiss his deputy for an offense and otherwise. And among (the rulings) is that when the judiciary is delegated to two (people), one of them may not undertake the judiciary (alone). So if they stipulate that each of them may act independently in the judiciary, there is no transmission (narration) on it, and Imam Zahir al-Din said: It should be permissible because the deputy of the judge is a deputy (na'ib) who is not dismissed by the dismissal of the judge and he possesses independence; thus in al-Bazzaziyyah.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings from the Hanafi sources al-Khulasah and al-Bazzaziyyah regarding the dismissal and delegation of judges. First, the sultan (the political ruler) has the authority to dismiss a judge, whether due to suspicion (raybah) of bias or corruption, or even without any specific cause. This underscores the sultan's general supervisory power over the judiciary. Second, the chief judge (qadi al-qudat) can dismiss his own deputy (na'ib) for a minor offense (jinhah) or for other reasons, indicating a hierarchical authority. Third, if the judicial authority is delegated to two persons (e.g., two judges for the same city), ordinarily one of them cannot act alone; they must act jointly. However, if they stipulate that each can act independently, the text says there is no established transmission (la riwayah fihi), meaning the earlier sources do not address this explicitly. Imam Zahir al-Din (a later Hanafi authority) opined that it should be permissible. His reasoning is that a deputy (na'ib) of a judge is not dismissed by the dismissal of the appointing judge and can possess independence (tafarud). This is quoted from al-Bazzaziyyah, stressing the deputy's independent capacity.

Key Terms
العزل (al-'azl): Dismissal of a judge from his position by the sultan or higher authority.
النائب (al-na'ib): A deputy judge appointed by the chief judge or sultan to perform judicial functions.
التفويض (al-tafwid): Delegation of judicial authority to someone else.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, can the sultan dismiss a judge without any suspicion (reiba)?
A) No, dismissal must always be based on suspicion of misconduct.
B) Yes, the sultan may dismiss the judge for suspicion or without suspicion.
C) Only the chief judge can dismiss a judge, not the sultan.
D) Dismissal without suspicion is not permissible but is enforced anyway.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants the sultan broad discretion to dismiss judges and defaults to joint action when delegating to two judges, though later scholars permit independent action by stipulation.

Suspension and Conditional Decrees in Judgeship
Rulings on conditional and suspended judgeship, and related cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on conditional judgeship, ratification of a fuduli's judgment, and the effect of time restrictions and exemptions on a judge's order.

Core Idea
Judgeship can be conditional, restricted, or ratified—each ruling turns on the presence or absence of the condition and jurisdiction.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents several key rulings concerning the appointment and decrees of a judge. It addresses whether judgeship can be suspended on a condition, how a judge's jurisdiction is determined, and what happens when a non-judge issues a ruling or when a judge acts outside his designated scope. These rulings highlight the Hanafi emphasis on legal validity tied to proper authority, conditions, and ratification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main topics
The passage covers four distinct rulings: (1) validity of suspending the appointment (ta'liq) and its default scope, (2) validity of conditional decrees by a judge, (3) ratification of a fuduli's judgment, and (4) consequences of judging outside appointed days or exempted cases.
Each ruling has its own condition and consequence.
2
Analyze the conditional appointment
The judge's appointment can be made conditional on a future event or restricted to a specific time or place. If no city is specified, he becomes judge of his own city, not the entire sultanate.
This clarifies the default jurisdiction.
3
Examine conditional decrees
A judge may phrase a decree conditionally, e.g., 'if you are his wife, I award you maintenance.' Such a decree is valid and takes effect when the condition is fulfilled, as shown in the maintenance case.
The condition must be a lawful matter.
4
Understand ratification and restrictions
A fuduli's judgment becomes valid upon ratification by the proper judge. If a judge is assigned specific days, his judgment on other days is suspended until ratified during his appointed time. Exempting certain cases renders any judgment in those cases void and unenforceable.
Ratification cures the defect of unauthorized judging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها صحة تعليقه وإضافته وتقييده بزمان ومكان ولو لم يقيده ببلد فالمختار أنه يصير قاضياً ببلده الذي هو فيه لا في كل بلاد السلطان وهذا في تعليق الولاية وهل يصح تعليق ولاية القضاء؟ قال في نفقات خزانة المفتين : امرأة أقامت على رجل بينة بالنكاح فلا نفقة لها في مدة المسألة عن الشهود ولو أراد القاضي أن يفرض لها النفقة لما رأى المصلحة أن يقول لها ينبغي إن كنت امرأته قد فرضت لك عليه في كل شهر كذا ويشهد على ذلك، فإذا مضى شهر وقد استدانت وعدلت البينة أخذت نفقتها منذ فرض لها اهـ. وعلى هذا فقول القاضي حكمت بكذا إن لم يمنع مانع شرعي صحيح. ومن أحكامه أنه لو قضى فضولي فأجاز القاضي قضاءه جاز، ولو كان مولى في كل أسبوع يومين فقضى في غير اليومين توقف قضاؤه، فإن أجازه في نوبته جاز كما في آخر جامع الفصولين؛ كذا في البزازية. ولو استثنى حوادث فلان لا يقضي فيها ولو قضى لا ينفذ.
And among them: the validity of suspending it (the judgeship), adding conditions, and restricting it to a time and place. Even if he does not restrict it to a specific city, the chosen view is that he becomes a judge of the city he is in, not in all territories of the sultan. And this concerns suspending the appointment. And is suspending the judgeship valid? He said in Nafaqat Khazanat al-Muftin: A woman presented evidence of marriage against a man, and she has no maintenance during the period of questioning the witnesses. If the judge wishes to order maintenance, when he sees the benefit, he should say to her: 'If you are his wife, I have prescribed for you from him each month such and such,' and have that witnessed. Then when a month passes and she has borrowed (money) and the evidence is adjusted (confirmed), she takes her maintenance from the time he prescribed for her. And accordingly, the statement of the judge: 'I have ruled such-and-such, unless a legal impediment prevents it' is valid. And among its rulings: if a non-judge (fuduli) gives a judgment and the judge ratifies it, it is permissible. And if a judge is assigned two days per week and he judges on other days, his judgment is suspended; if he ratifies it during his appointed time, it becomes permissible, as in the end of Jami' al-Fusulayn; thus in al-Bazzaziyyah. And if he exempts the incidents of so-and-so, he should not judge in them, and if he judges, it is not executed.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage discusses various rulings related to the judge's appointment and decrees. First, the appointment of a judge can be made conditional (ta'liq) and can be restricted by time and place. If no specific place is mentioned, he becomes judge of his town of residence, not of the entire sultanate. Then the question arises: Is it valid to suspend the judgeship itself? The author quotes from Nafaqat Khazanat al-Muftin a case where a judge may postpone a maintenance award until the marriage is confirmed. This illustrates a conditional decree: the judge says 'if you are his wife, I prescribe maintenance' and later when confirmed, it takes effect from the prescription date. Similarly, any judge's statement like 'I have ruled unless a legal impediment prevents it' is valid, meaning it becomes a binding judgment once the impediment is removed. Next, if a person not officially a judge (fuduli) issues a judgment and the proper judge later ratifies it, it becomes valid. Also, if a judge is appointed to sit on certain days only and he judges on other days, his judgments are suspended until he ratifies them on his proper day. Finally, if the judge exempts certain cases (e.g., cases involving a particular person) from his jurisdiction, he cannot judge those cases; if he does, the judgment is not enforceable. These rulings emphasize the importance of jurisdiction, conditions, and ratification in ensuring the validity of judicial acts.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending the effectiveness of the judgeship or decree on a future condition.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): An unauthorized person who acts without legal capacity, but whose actions can be ratified.
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Exempting certain cases from the judge's jurisdiction by condition or instruction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a judge is told to judge only on two days per week but he gives a judgment on another day, what happens?
A) The judgment is completely void.
B) The judgment is suspended until he ratifies it during his appointed time.
C) The judgment is valid immediately.
D) The judgment becomes valid only if the sultan confirms it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, judicial acts can be conditional, restricted, or ratified; understanding these rules ensures that a judge's orders are valid and enforceable.

Deputy Appointment, Duration, and Scope of Judgment
Rulings on the judge's deputy appointment, term, and extent of verdict

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rulings on a judge's authority in Hanafi fiqh: deputy appointment, duration, and scope of judgment, and identify which matters are binding on all parties.

Core Idea
A judge's verdict binds the litigants, not all Muslims, except for rulings on freedom, lineage, marriage, and wala'.

This lesson addresses key principles governing a judge's authority in Hanafi law. It covers when a judge may appoint a deputy, how long he should remain in office, and the extent to which his judgment affects others. Understanding these rules is essential for grasping the limits of judicial power in Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
Read the passage and list the three distinct rules: (1) conditions for deputy appointment, (2) recommended tenure, (3) scope of judgment.
Each ruling is introduced with 'ومنها' (and among them).
2
Analyze the exception for deputy appointment
Note that a judge cannot delegate unless given explicit or implicit permission, such as being named 'chief judge'.
This ensures accountability and prevents unauthorized delegation.
3
Consider the rationale for the one-year tenure
The text says 'so as not to forget knowledge.' This is a recommended limitation to maintain scholarly freshness.
It is not a hard legal requirement but a guidance.
4
Distinguish between binding and non-binding judgments
The general rule: judgment binds only the parties and those deriving title. Exceptions: freedom, nasab, wala', marriage – these bind everyone.
Exceptions are matters of personal status that affect the community.
5
Apply the scope rule to waqf
Unlike the exceptions, a judgment on waqf does not extend universally; it binds only the litigants. This is the soundest view.
This is because waqf is considered a private right, not a public status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنها لا يملك الاستخلاف إلا بإذن صريح أو دلالة بأن يقول له جعلتك قاضي القضاة ومنها أن القاضي لا يبقى أكثر من سنة كي لا ينسى العلم. ومنها أنه يقتصر على المقضى عليه وعلى كل من تلقى الملك منه ولا يتعدى إلى الكافة ويتعدى في القضاء بالحرية والنسب والولاء والنكاح، ولا يتعدى في الوقف على الأصح وقدمناه في باب الاستحقاق من البيوع
And among them: that he does not possess the power to appoint a deputy (istikhalf) except with explicit permission or indication, such as by saying to him: 'I have made you a chief judge.' And among them: that the judge should not remain (in office) for more than a year, lest he forget knowledge. And among them: that (the effect of his judgment) is restricted to the one against whom judgment is given and to everyone who takes ownership from him, and it does not extend to the general public. But it does extend in judgments concerning freedom, lineage, patronage (wala'), and marriage, and it does not extend in the case of waqf, according to the soundest view, and we have presented this earlier in the chapter on pre-emption from the book of sales.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage provides three important rulings regarding a judge's authority. First, a judge cannot appoint a deputy (na'ib or khalifah) to act in his place unless he has explicit permission from the appointing authority (the sultan or chief judge) or an indication that implies such authority, like being told 'I have made you a chief judge' (qadi al-qudat), which implies the power to appoint others. Second, it is recommended that a judge should not remain in his position for more than one year, so as not to become complacent and forget the knowledge of fiqh. This is a guideline for rotating judgeship to maintain fresh scholarship. Third, the effect of a judge's verdict generally binds only the parties to the case (the one against whom judgment is given and those deriving title from him), not the general public. However, there are exceptions: judgments concerning freedom (e.g., declaring a slave free), lineage (nasab), patronage (wala' — the relationship of clientage), and marriage (nikah) are effective against all (kafah). In contrast, judgments about endowments (waqf) do not have this extensive effect, according to the soundest view (al-ashahh), and this has been discussed earlier in the chapter on pre-emption.

Key Terms
الاستخلاف (al-istikhalf): The act of appointing a deputy judge to act on one's behalf.
التعدي (al-ta'addi): The extension of a judgment's effect beyond the immediate parties to affect the general public.
الوقف (al-waqf): An endowment made for charitable or family purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It extends to the general public like freedom and marriage.
B) It does not extend to the general public; it binds only the parties.
C) It extends only if the waqf is public.
D) It extends only if the judge explicitly states so.

Key Takeaway
The effect of a judge's ruling is generally limited to the litigants, with exceptions for personal status matters like freedom, lineage, marriage, and patronage.

Automatic Removal: Loss of Senses and Religion
Ruling on automatic removal by physical and spiritual defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four characteristics that automatically remove a Hanafi judge and explain the later nuance regarding apostasy.

Core Idea
Loss of hearing, sight, reason, or religion automatically removes a judge, but later fatwa holds apostasy does not remove the office.

This lesson examines the initial ruling from al-Bazzaziyyah that a judge is automatically removed by four defects. However, later Hanafi sources introduce a critical nuance regarding apostasy, which this lesson unpacks by distinguishing removal from office from invalidity of judgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four characteristics
Read the opening of al-Bazzaziyyah: 'four characteristics if they occur to the judge, he is removed: loss of hearing, sight, intellect, or religion.'
These are physical/spiritual incapacities that make judging impossible.
2
Note the apparent contradiction on apostasy
Later, al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyyah states the fatwa is that apostasy does not remove the judge, and al-Walwalijiyyah says the apostate judge remains when he reforms.
This appears to conflict with 'loss of religion' as an automatic remover.
3
Resolve by distinguishing office from judgments
The resolution is that apostasy removes the judge's authority to execute judgments (nufudh), but not the office itself. Thus, he is 'removed' from effective judging but not from tenure.
The text says 'فوات الدين' may refer to execution, not office.
4
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that apostasy does not remove the judge; his judgments during apostasy are void, but he remains in office until dismissal or repentance.
This differs from other schools that hold apostasy terminates the appointment immediately.
5
Apply the ruling on recovery
If a judge becomes blind and later regains sight, he remains in his position (al-Khizanah).
Similarly, if a judge loses religion and repents, he continues without reappointment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع فيما يخرج القاضي عن القضاء ففي البزازية : أربع خصال إذا حل بالقاضي انعزل فوات السمع أو البصر أو العقل أو الدين. وإذا عزل السلطان القاضي لا ينعزل ما لم يصل إليه الخبر كالوكيل. وعن الثاني أنه لا ينعزل ما لم يأت قاض آخر صيانة للمسلمين عن تعطيل قضاياهم . وهذا إذا لم يعلق عزله بشرط كوصول الكتاب ونحوه وإن معلقاً لا ينعزل ما لم يصل إليه الكتاب وإن وصل إليه الخبر. وإذا مات القاضي انعزل خلفاؤه، وإذا عزل القاضي فالفتوى على أن النائب لا ينعزل بعزله لأنه نائب السلطان أو العامة وبعزل نائب القاضي لا ينعزل القاضي ولا ينعزل بموت الخليفة؛ كذا في البزازية وفيها القاضي إذا عزل نفسه وبلغ السلطان عزله ينعزل، وكذا إذا كتب به إلى السلطان وبلغ الكتاب إلى السلطان، وقيل لا ينعزل بعزل نفسه لأنه نائب عن العامة فلا يملك إبطال حقهم ا هـ. وينبغي أن الخصم لو علم بعزله ولم يعلم القاضي أنه لا ينفذ حكمه لعلمه أنه غير حاكم باطناً ولم أره، وكذا لم أر ما إذا بلغ النائب عزل قاضي القضاة، وينبغي أن لا ينعزل حتى يعلم أصله، وكذا لم أر حكم ما إذا بلغ الأصل دون النواب ولم يعلمهم فحكموا وينبغي أن يصح حكمهم وأن يستحق الأصل ما عين له على القضاء من بيت المال المباشرة نوابه وفي البدائع أن القاضي يخرج عن القضاء بكل ما يخرج الوكيل إلا إذا مات الخليفة أو خلع فإنه لا تنعزل قضاته وولاته، وإذا مات الموكل انعزل وكيله ولا ينعزل بأخذ الرشوة والفسق عندنا ا هـ . البزازية : قلد السلطان رجلاً قضاء بلدة ثم بعد أيام قلد القضاء آخر ولم يتعرض لعزل الأول الأظهر والأشبه أنه لا ينعزل اهـ. وفي الولوالجية : إذا ارتد القاضي أو فسق ثم صلح فهو على حاله لأن المرتد أمره موقوف ولأن الارتداد فسق وبنفس الفسق لا ينعزل إلا أن ما قضى في حالة الردة باطل بخلاف الحكم إذا ارتد فإنه يخرج والفرق مذكور فيها، وما قدمناه أنه ينعزل بفوات الدين يخالفه إلا أن يقال بالردة ينعزل عن نفاذ قضائه جمعاً البزازية من بينهما. وفي الواقعات الحسامية : الفتوى على أنه لا ينعزل بالردة فإن الكفر لا ينافي ابتداء القضاء في إحدى الروايتين حتى لو قلد الكافر ثم أسلم هل يحتاج إلى تقليد آخرء فيه روايتان ا هـ. وبه علمت أن ما في الخلاصة على خلاف المفتى به وعلمت أن تقليد الكافر صحيح وإن لم يصح قضاؤه على المسلم حال كفره. وفي الخزانة : إذا عمي القاضي ثم أبصر فهو على قضائه ا هـ .
The ninth (section): On what removes the judge from the judiciary. In al-Bazzaziyyah: Four characteristics: if they occur to the judge, he is removed: loss of hearing, or sight, or reason, or religion. And if the sultan dismisses the judge, he is not removed until the news reaches him, like an agent (wakil). And according to a second (view), he is not removed until another judge comes, to safeguard Muslims from stagnation of their judiciary. This is if his dismissal is not conditioned upon something like the arrival of a letter and the like, but if it is conditional, he is not removed until the letter reaches him, even if the news reaches him. When the judge dies, his deputies (khulafa') are removed. And when the judge is dismissed, the fatwa is that the deputy (na'ib) is not removed by his dismissal, because he is a deputy of the sultan or the public. And with the dismissal of the deputy of the judge, the judge is not removed, nor is he removed by the death of the caliph. Thus in al-Bazzaziyyah. And in it: If the judge dismisses himself and the sultan is informed of his dismissal, he is removed. Similarly, if he writes about it to the sultan and the letter reaches the sultan. And it is said he is not removed by his own dismissal because he is a deputy of the public and cannot annul their right. It is appropriate that if the litigant knows of his dismissal but the judge does not, his judgment should not be executed because he knows he is not a true judge, but I have not seen that. Similarly, I have not seen (a ruling) regarding when the deputy learns of the dismissal of the chief judge; it is appropriate that he is not removed until he knows of his origin. Similarly, I have not seen the ruling if the origin (chief) learns but the deputies do not, and they judge; it is appropriate that their judgment is valid and that the origin (chief) is entitled to what was assigned to him for the judiciary from the treasury through the direct work of his deputies. And in al-Bada'i': The judge is removed from the judiciary by everything that removes an agent (wakil), except that if the caliph dies or is deposed, his judges and governors are not removed. And if the principal (muwakkil) dies, his agent is removed. And he is not removed by bribery or immorality (fisq) according to us. Thus in al-Bazzaziyyah: The sultan appointed a man to the judiciary of a town, then after some days appointed another to the judiciary without addressing the dismissal of the first; the most apparent (al-azhar) and most similar (al-ashbah) is that he is not removed. In al-Walwalijiyyah: If the judge apostatizes or becomes immoral (fasiq) and then reforms, he remains as he was, because the apostate's affair is suspended, and because apostasy is immorality, and by mere immorality he is not removed, except that what he judged during the state of apostasy is void. This differs from the judge (the one who judges) if he apostatizes, for he leaves (the judiciary). The difference is mentioned there. And what we previously mentioned—that he is removed by loss of religion—contradicts this, unless it is said that by apostasy he is removed from the execution of his judgments, combining between them. In al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyyah: The fatwa is that he is not removed by apostasy, because disbelief does not preclude the initiation of judgeship in one of the two narrations. Even if a disbeliever is appointed and then becomes Muslim, is a new appointment needed? There are two narrations. From this you know that what is in al-Khulasah is contrary to the fatwa, and you know that the appointment of a disbeliever is valid, even if his judgment over a Muslim is not valid during his disbelief. In al-Khizanah: If the judge becomes blind and then regains sight, he remains in his judgeship.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This extensive passage collects numerous rulings from various sources on the causes that remove a judge from his position. It begins by quoting al-Bazzaziyyah on four physical or spiritual defects that automatically remove a judge: loss of hearing, sight, intellect, or religion (i.e., apostasy). However, later sources nuance this, especially regarding apostasy. If the sultan dismisses a judge, the removal takes effect only when the judge receives the notice, like an agent's appointment terminates upon knowledge. Some hold that it takes effect only when another judge arrives to prevent a vacuum. If the dismissal is conditional (e.g., upon receipt of a letter), it takes effect only when the condition is fulfilled. A judge's death removes his deputies, but if a judge is dismissed, his deputies are not removed because they are considered deputies of the sultan or the public, not of the judge. Similarly, dismissal of a deputy does not affect the judge. The judge's resignation becomes effective when the sultan knows; an independent view says resignation is ineffective because the judge is a deputy of the public. An obscure scenario: if a litigant knows of dismissal but the judge does not, the judgment should not be enforced, but the author notes this is not explicitly stated. The author adds his own reasoning on various scenarios. From al-Bada'i': a judge is removed by the same things as an agent, except the caliph's death or removal. Bribery and immorality do not remove a judge according to the Hanafi school. If the sultan appoints two judges separately without dismissing the first, the first remains. Apostasy or immorality does not remove the judge, but judgments during apostasy are void; there is a contradiction with earlier loss of religion, which is resolved by saying loss of religion removes from execution, not the office itself. The fatwa from al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyyah is that apostasy does not remove the judge, based on the ruling that a disbeliever can be initially appointed, though his judgments over Muslims are invalid. Thus, appointing a disbeliever is valid. Finally, blindness and recovery do not remove the judge.

Key Terms
الانعزال (al-in'izal): Automatic removal of the judge from office by operation of law upon occurrence of certain defects.
فوات الدين (fawat al-din): Loss of religion; apostasy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later fatwa in al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyyah, what is the effect of apostasy on a Hanafi judge?
A) The judge is automatically removed from office.
B) The judge remains in office but his judgments during apostasy are void.
C) The judge must be formally dismissed by the sultan.
D) The judge is removed only if he does not repent within three days.

Key Takeaway
Loss of hearing, sight, or intellect automatically removes a judge, but apostasy, per later fatwa, does not remove the office—only invalidates judgments.

Dismissal by the Sultan and Resignation: The Knowledge Requirement
Rulings on the effectiveness of dismissal and resignation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that dismissal and resignation take effect only upon knowledge of the other party, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of resignation.

Core Idea
A judge is not removed by dismissal or resignation until the news reaches the other party, but some hold resignation is ineffective.

This lesson focuses on the procedural aspects of judicial removal: the sultan's dismissal and the judge's resignation. The Hanafi school analogizes to the law of agency (wakalah), requiring knowledge for effectiveness. There is also a notable dispute on whether a judge can resign at all, given his role as a deputy of the public.

Guided Path
1
State the rule on sultan's dismissal
The text says: 'If the sultan dismisses the judge, he is not removed until the news reaches him, like an agent.' This means dismissal takes effect upon the judge's knowledge, not upon the sultan's act.
Analogy to agent (wakil): an agent's appointment ends when he learns of termination.
2
Note the second view on timing
A second view holds the judge is not removed until another judge actually arrives, to prevent a vacuum in the judiciary.
This practical consideration overrides the general agency rule.
3
Examine conditional dismissal
If dismissal is conditioned (e.g., upon receipt of a letter), it takes effect only when the condition is met, even if news arrives earlier.
This shows the school's emphasis on certainty of knowledge.
4
Analyze resignation (self-dismissal)
The text presents two views: (a) if the judge resigns and the sultan knows, he is removed; (b) some say resignation is ineffective because the judge is a deputy of the public and cannot annul their right.
The mufta bihi is that resignation with the sultan's knowledge is effective.
5
Apply to deputies and other scenarios
The judge's dismissal does not remove his deputies, as they are deputies of the sultan or public. Death of the caliph does not remove the judge.
The deputy's connection is to the sultan, not the appointing judge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع فيما يخرج القاضي عن القضاء ففي البزازية : أربع خصال إذا حل بالقاضي انعزل فوات السمع أو البصر أو العقل أو الدين. وإذا عزل السلطان القاضي لا ينعزل ما لم يصل إليه الخبر كالوكيل. وعن الثاني أنه لا ينعزل ما لم يأت قاض آخر صيانة للمسلمين عن تعطيل قضاياهم . وهذا إذا لم يعلق عزله بشرط كوصول الكتاب ونحوه وإن معلقاً لا ينعزل ما لم يصل إليه الكتاب وإن وصل إليه الخبر. وإذا مات القاضي انعزل خلفاؤه، وإذا عزل القاضي فالفتوى على أن النائب لا ينعزل بعزله لأنه نائب السلطان أو العامة وبعزل نائب القاضي لا ينعزل القاضي ولا ينعزل بموت الخليفة؛ كذا في البزازية وفيها القاضي إذا عزل نفسه وبلغ السلطان عزله ينعزل، وكذا إذا كتب به إلى السلطان وبلغ الكتاب إلى السلطان، وقيل لا ينعزل بعزل نفسه لأنه نائب عن العامة فلا يملك إبطال حقهم ا هـ. وينبغي أن الخصم لو علم بعزله ولم يعلم القاضي أنه لا ينفذ حكمه لعلمه أنه غير حاكم باطناً ولم أره، وكذا لم أر ما إذا بلغ النائب عزل قاضي القضاة، وينبغي أن لا ينعزل حتى يعلم أصله، وكذا لم أر حكم ما إذا بلغ الأصل دون النواب ولم يعلمهم فحكموا وينبغي أن يصح حكمهم وأن يستحق الأصل ما عين له على القضاء من بيت المال المباشرة نوابه وفي البدائع أن القاضي يخرج عن القضاء بكل ما يخرج الوكيل إلا إذا مات الخليفة أو خلع فإنه لا تنعزل قضاته وولاته، وإذا مات الموكل انعزل وكيله ولا ينعزل بأخذ الرشوة والفسق عندنا ا هـ . البزازية : قلد السلطان رجلاً قضاء بلدة ثم بعد أيام قلد القضاء آخر ولم يتعرض لعزل الأول الأظهر والأشبه أنه لا ينعزل اهـ. وفي الولوالجية : إذا ارتد القاضي أو فسق ثم صلح فهو على حاله لأن المرتد أمره موقوف ولأن الارتداد فسق وبنفس الفسق لا ينعزل إلا أن ما قضى في حالة الردة باطل بخلاف الحكم إذا ارتد فإنه يخرج والفرق مذكور فيها، وما قدمناه أنه ينعزل بفوات الدين يخالفه إلا أن يقال بالردة ينعزل عن نفاذ قضائه جمعاً البزازية من بينهما. وفي الواقعات الحسامية : الفتوى على أنه لا ينعزل بالردة فإن الكفر لا ينافي ابتداء القضاء في إحدى الروايتين حتى لو قلد الكافر ثم أسلم هل يحتاج إلى تقليد آخرء فيه روايتان ا هـ. وبه علمت أن ما في الخلاصة على خلاف المفتى به وعلمت أن تقليد الكافر صحيح وإن لم يصح قضاؤه على المسلم حال كفره. وفي الخزانة : إذا عمي القاضي ثم أبصر فهو على قضائه ا هـ .
The ninth (section): On what removes the judge from the judiciary. In al-Bazzaziyyah: Four characteristics: if they occur to the judge, he is removed: loss of hearing, or sight, or reason, or religion. And if the sultan dismisses the judge, he is not removed until the news reaches him, like an agent (wakil). And according to a second (view), he is not removed until another judge comes, to safeguard Muslims from stagnation of their judiciary. This is if his dismissal is not conditioned upon something like the arrival of a letter and the like, but if it is conditional, he is not removed until the letter reaches him, even if the news reaches him. When the judge dies, his deputies (khulafa') are removed. And when the judge is dismissed, the fatwa is that the deputy (na'ib) is not removed by his dismissal, because he is a deputy of the sultan or the public. And with the dismissal of the deputy of the judge, the judge is not removed, nor is he removed by the death of the caliph. Thus in al-Bazzaziyyah. It is appropriate that if the litigant knows of his dismissal but the judge does not, his judgment should not be executed because he knows he is not a true judge, but I have not seen that. Similarly, I have not seen (a ruling) regarding when the deputy learns of the dismissal of the chief judge; it is appropriate that he is not removed until he knows of his origin. Similarly, I have not seen the ruling if the origin (chief) learns but the deputies do not, and they judge; it is appropriate that their judgment is valid and that the origin (chief) is entitled to what was assigned to him for the judiciary from the treasury through the direct work of his deputies. Checkpoint
A sultan dismisses a judge by letter. The letter reaches the judge's office but he is on leave and does not see it for a week. During that week, the deputy judge presides. Are the deputy's rulings valid?
A) No, because the dismissal was effective when the letter arrived.
B) Yes, because the judge must personally know of the dismissal.
C) No, because the deputy is automatically removed when the judge is dismissed.
D) Yes, because the deputy is not removed by the judge's dismissal (per the fatwa).

Bribery, Fisq, and Apostasy: Non-Removing Factors
Rulings on non-removing factors: bribery, fisq, and apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that bribery and fisq do not remove a Hanafi judge, and explain the distinction between the effect of apostasy on the office and on judgments.

Core Idea
Bribery and immorality do not remove a judge; apostasy suspends judgments but does not end tenure.

This lesson examines three factors that many might expect to disqualify a judge but, according to the Hanafi school, do not remove him from office: bribery, fisq (immorality), and apostasy. The school draws a careful line between the judge's tenure and the validity of his actions. Apostasy is particularly nuanced: while it invalidates judgments, the judge remains in office until dismissal or repentance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on bribery and fisq
From al-Bada'i': 'ولا ينعزل بأخذ الرشوة والفسق عندنا' — he is not removed by bribery or immorality according to us.
This is a distinctive Hanafi position; other schools may remove him.
2
Note the fatwa on apostasy
From al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyyah: 'الفتوى على أنه لا ينعزل بالردة' — the fatwa is that he is not removed by apostasy.
This overrides the earlier statement that loss of religion removes.
3
Understand the distinction between office and execution
The earlier contradiction is resolved by saying apostasy removes the judge's authority to execute judgments (nufudh), not the office itself. His judgments during apostasy are void, but he remains as judge.
The text says: 'بأنه ينعزل عن نفاذ قضائه' — he is removed from the execution of his judgments.
4
Examine the case of an apostate who reforms
From al-Walwalijiyyah: 'إذا ارتد القاضي أو فسق ثم صلح فهو على حاله' — if the judge apostatizes or becomes immoral and then reforms, he remains as he was.
Thus, no reappointment is needed after repentance.
5
Apply the ruling on appointment of a disbeliever
The text states that appointing a disbeliever as judge is valid, though his judgment over a Muslim is not valid during his disbelief. Upon embracing Islam, he continues without new appointment in one narration.
This reinforces that disbelief does not terminate the office; it only affects judgment validity.

Source Text
