If the dismissal is conditional (e.g., upon receipt of a letter), it takes effect only when the condition is fulfilled. A judge's death removes his deputies, but if a judge is dismissed, his deputies are not removed because they are considered deputies of the sultan or the public, not of the judge. Similarly, dismissal of a deputy does not affect the judge. The judge's resignation becomes effective when the sultan knows; an independent view says resignation is ineffective because the judge is a deputy of the public. An obscure scenario: if a litigant knows of dismissal but the judge does not, the judgment should not be enforced, but the author notes this is not explicitly stated. The author adds his own reasoning on various scenarios. From al-Bada'i': a judge is removed by the same things as an agent, except the caliph's death or removal. Bribery and immorality do not remove a judge according to the Hanafi school. If the sultan appoints two judges separately without dismissing the first, the first remains. Apostasy or immorality does not remove the judge, but judgments during apostasy are void; there is a contradiction with earlier loss of religion, which is resolved by saying loss of religion removes from execution, not the office itself. The fatwa from al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyyah is that apostasy does not remove the judge, based on the ruling that a disbeliever can be initially appointed, though his judgments over Muslims are invalid. Thus, appointing a disbeliever is valid. Finally, blindness and recovery do not remove the judge.

Key Terms
الرشوة (al-rishwah): Bribery; giving or taking of a bribe.
الفسق (al-fisq): Immorality; sinfulness that does not reach apostasy.
الردة (al-riddah): Apostasy from Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of bribery on a judge's tenure?
A) The judge is automatically removed from office.
B) The judge is not removed; only the sultan can dismiss him.
C) The judge must be removed by the chief justice.
D) The judge is removed only if the bribe is proven in court.

Key Takeaway
Bribery and fisq do not remove a Hanafi judge; apostasy does not remove the office but voids judgments.

The Ethical Foundations of Judging: Justice, Rights, and Enjoining Good
Opening of the ninth section on the judge's etiquette

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three core ethical duties of a judge as outlined in the Hanafi tradition and explain why commanding good and forbidding evil is considered among the greatest acts of worship.

Core Idea
A judge's role is not merely procedural; it is an act of worship rooted in prophetic practice.

This passage opens the section on the judge's etiquette (adab) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It immediately grounds the judge's role in a spiritual and ethical framework, listing three core duties: ensuring justice, delivering rights, and the special obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil. These are not optional virtues but integral to the office of qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three duties
Read the passage and list the three ethical duties the author mentions for a judge: insaf al-mazlum, takhlis al-huquq, and amr bi'l-ma'ruf wa nahy 'an al-munkar.
These duties are not exhaustive but are highlighted as foundational.
2
Note the emphasis on commanding good and forbidding evil
The author explicitly says this is 'among the greatest acts of worship' and that every prophet was commanded with it. Recognize the elevated status.
This sets a high spiritual bar for the judge.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
The verse from Surah al-Ma'idah (5:44) is quoted. Understand its context: it shows that prophets judged by divine guidance. How does this support the judge's duties?
The Hanafi school uses this as a dalil for the judge's obligation to uphold justice and enjoin good.
4
Connect the judge's authority to prophetic tradition
The judge inherits the prophetic mission of commanding good and forbidding evil. This elevates judicial work from a mere profession to a form of worship.
This connection is unique in emphasizing the spiritual dimension of judging.

Automatic Dismissal of the Qadi and Effect of Ruler's Dismissal
Ruling on automatic dismissal conditions and effects of ruler's dismissal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the causes that automatically dismiss a qadi and explain when a ruler's dismissal takes effect, including the condition of knowledge.

Core Idea
A qadi is automatically dismissed by losing hearing, sight, intellect, or faith; ruler's dismissal requires news reaching him.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the rules governing a qadi's dismissal. It distinguishes between automatic dismissal due to loss of qualifications and dismissal by the ruler, which requires actual knowledge. The text also presents an alternative opinion aimed at preventing disruption of judicial services.

Guided Path
1
Identify automatic dismissal causes
List the four conditions that automatically remove a qadi: loss of hearing, sight, intellect, or apostasy.
These are physical and spiritual incapacities.
2
Understand ruler's dismissal rule
The ruler's dismissal takes effect only when the qadi receives news, like an agent (wakil).
3
Examine the second opinion
Some scholars say dismissal only occurs when a replacement arrives, to avoid interruption of cases.
This is a protective measure.
4
Consider conditional dismissals
If dismissal is tied to a condition (e.g., arrival of a letter), it takes effect only upon fulfillment of condition, not mere news.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا حل بالقاضي انعزل فوات السمع أو البصر أو العقل أو الدين. وإذا عزل السلطان القاضي لا ينعزل ما لم يصل إليه الخبر كالوكيل. وعن الثاني أنه لا ينعزل ما لم يأت قاض آخر صيانة للمسلمين عن تعطيل قضاياهم . وهذا إذا لم يعلق عزله بشرط كوصول الكتاب ونحوه وإن معلقاً لا ينعزل ما لم يصل إليه الكتاب وإن وصل إليه الخبر.
When it becomes necessary for the qadi, he is dismissed by the loss of hearing, sight, intellect, or religion. And when the ruler dismisses the qadi, he is not dismissed unless the news reaches him, like the wakil. And according to the second (opinion), he is not dismissed until another qadi comes, to protect Muslims from neglect of their cases. And this is if his dismissal is not attached to a condition like the arrival of the book and the like; if it is conditional, he is not dismissed until the book reaches him, even if the news reaches him.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the causes of automatic dismissal for a qadi: loss of hearing, sight, intellect, or religion (apostasy). Then it addresses when the ruler directly dismisses the qadi: the dismissal does not take effect until the qadi receives the news, analogous to a wakil (agent). There is a second opinion that he is not dismissed until a replacement qadi is appointed, to prevent disruption of judicial services. Finally, if the dismissal is conditional (e.g., 'you are dismissed when this letter arrives'), the qadi is not dismissed until the condition is fulfilled, regardless of whether he hears the news.

Key Terms
عزل (‘azl): The removal of a qadi from his position by the ruler or by automatic causes.
قاضي (qadi): The judge appointed by the ruler to adjudicate disputes.
وكيل (wakil): An agent; here analogized to the qadi regarding the effect of dismissal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) When the news reaches the qadi
C) Only when a new qadi is appointed
D) At the end of the judicial term

Key Takeaway
A qadi is automatically dismissed by loss of essential qualifications; ruler's dismissal requires actual knowledge to take effect, with a protective alternative opinion.

Rulings on the Qadi's Death, Dismissal, and Self-Resignation
Ruling statements on various scenarios of qadi dismissal, deputy status, and self-resignation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the effects of a qadi's death and dismissal on deputies, and explain when a qadi's self-resignation takes effect.

Core Idea
A qadi's deputy is appointed by the ruler/public, not the qadi, so the deputy is not dismissed when the qadi is dismissed.

The office of qadi involves complex relationships with deputies and appointing authorities. This lesson clarifies what happens when a qadi dies, is dismissed, or resigns, drawing from the Hanafi commentary al-Bahr al-Raiq. Understanding these rules ensures that judicial authority remains properly defined and that judgments are valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering events
List the events discussed: death of qadi, dismissal of qadi, dismissal of deputy, death of caliph, self-resignation of qadi, appointment of second qadi.
Each event has a distinct effect on judicial authority.
2
State each ruling concisely
For each event, note the legal consequence as given in the text. For example: upon qadi's death, deputies are dismissed; upon qadi's dismissal, deputies remain.
Pay attention to the fatwa on deputy status.
3
Identify the underlying principle
The key principle is that the deputy represents the ruler or the public, not the qadi personally. Hence, the deputy's authority is independent of the qadi's appointment.
This explains why deputy is not dismissed when qadi is dismissed.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on self-resignation
Two views: (1) valid upon notification to ruler; (2) invalid because qadi is deputy of the public. Note which is preferred.
The text implies the first view is the fatwa.
5
Compare with the wakil analogy
The qadi leaves office for all reasons that dismiss a wakil, except death/deposition of caliph (qadi stays). A wakil is dismissed by principal's death; a qadi is not dismissed by bribery or fisq.
This highlights the unique public nature of the qadi's office.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا مات القاضي انعزل خلفاؤه، وإذا عزل القاضي فالفتوى على أن النائب لا ينعزل بعزله لأنه نائب السلطان أو العامة وبعزل نائب القاضي لا ينعزل القاضي ولا ينعزل بموت الخليفة؛ كذا في البزازية وفيها القاضي إذا عزل نفسه وبلغ السلطان عزله ينعزل، وكذا إذا كتب به إلى السلطان وبلغ الكتاب إلى السلطان، وقيل لا ينعزل بعزل نفسه لأنه نائب عن العامة فلا يملك إبطال حقهم ا هـ. وينبغي أن الخصم لو علم بعزله ولم يعلم القاضي أنه لا ينفذ حكمه لعلمه أنه غير حاكم باطناً ولم أره، وكذا لم أر ما إذا بلغ النائب عزل قاضي القضاة، وينبغي أن لا ينعزل حتى يعلم أصله، وكذا لم أر حكم ما إذا بلغ الأصل دون النواب ولم يعلمهم فحكموا وينبغي أن يصح حكمهم وأن يستحق الأصل ما عين له على القضاء من بيت المال المباشرة نوابه وفي البدائع أن القاضي يخرج عن القضاء بكل ما يخرج الوكيل إلا إذا مات الخليفة أو خلع فإنه لا تنعزل قضاته وولاته، وإذا مات الموكل انعزل وكيله ولا ينعزل بأخذ الرشوة والفسق عندنا ا هـ . البزازية : قلد السلطان رجلاً قضاء بلدة ثم بعد أيام قلد القضاء آخر ولم يتعرض لعزل الأول الأظهر والأشبه أنه لا ينعزل اهـ.
And when the qadi dies, his successors are dismissed. And when the qadi is dismissed, the fatwa is that the deputy is not dismissed by his dismissal because he is a deputy of the ruler or the public. And by the dismissal of the qadi's deputy, the qadi is not dismissed. Nor is he dismissed by the death of the caliph; as in al-Bazzaziyya. And in it: If the qadi dismisses himself and the ruler's dismissal reaches him, he is dismissed. Similarly, if he writes to the ruler and the letter reaches the ruler. And it is said he is not dismissed by his own dismissal because he is a deputy of the public and cannot annul their right. It is fitting that if the opponent knows of his dismissal but the qadi does not know, his judgment is not enforceable because he knows he is not a ruler inwardly, but I have not seen it. Similarly, I have not seen the case when the news of the dismissal of the chief qadi reaches the deputy; it is fitting that he is not dismissed until he knows his origin. Similarly, I have not seen the ruling when the news reaches the principal but not the deputies, and they judge; it is fitting that their judgment is valid and the principal is entitled to what was allocated for him from the treasury, though the deputies are direct. In al-Bada'i': The qadi leaves the office by everything that removes a wakil, except if the caliph dies or is deposed, then his qadis and governors are not dismissed. And when the principal dies, his wakil is dismissed. And he is not dismissed by taking bribes or fisq, according to us. Al-Bazzaziyya: If the ruler appoints a man as judge of a town, then after some days appoints another judge without mentioning the dismissal of the first, the most apparent and appropriate is that he is not dismissed.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage deals with various scenarios of dismissal. First, upon the death of the qadi, his deputies (khalafa) are automatically dismissed. When the qadi is dismissed by the ruler, his deputy is not dismissed because the deputy is appointed by the ruler or the public directly. Conversely, dismissing the deputy does not dismiss the qadi. The qadi is also not dismissed by the death of the caliph (ruler). Regarding self-resignation, if the qadi resigns and the ruler is informed, he is dismissed. There is an opinion that he cannot resign because he represents the public. The author discusses theoretical cases: if a litigant knows of the qadi's dismissal but the qadi does not, the judgment is void, but he hasn't seen this reported. If the deputy hears of the dismissal of the chief qadi, he is not dismissed until he learns from his source. If the principal hears of his own dismissal but deputies do not, and they judge, their judgments are valid. The qadi's entitlement to salary from the public treasury remains even if he delegates. Drawing from al-Bada'i', the qadi leaves office for all reasons that remove a wakil, except the death or deposition of the caliph—then the qadi and governors remain. A wakil is dismissed by the death of the principal, but a qadi is not dismissed by taking bribes or committing fisq (sin) in our school. Finally, if the ruler appoints a second judge without dismissing the first, the first is not dismissed.

Key Terms
نائب (na’ib): Deputy judge appointed by the qadi or ruler.
خليفة (khalifa): The caliph or supreme ruler.
عزل (azl): Dismissal or removal from office.
قاضي (qadi): Judge appointed to adjudicate disputes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The deputy is also dismissed
B) The deputy remains in office
C) The deputy becomes the new qadi
D) The deputy must be removed by the ruler

Key Takeaway
The deputy's authority depends on the ruler/public, not on the qadi, so it continues after the qadi's dismissal but ends with the qadi's death.

Does Apostasy Automatically Dismiss the Qadi?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on apostasy and fisq of the qadi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the effect of apostasy on a qadi's office and distinguish between the mufta bihi and alternative narrations within the school.

Core Idea
Apostasy does not dismiss the qadi; it only voids his judgments during that period.

Within the Hanafi school, there is a dispute whether a qadi is automatically dismissed upon apostasy or committing fisq (sinfulness). Some sources, like al-Khulasa, suggest dismissal due to 'loss of religion.' However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) from al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyya holds that apostasy does not terminate the office; it only nullifies the qadi's rulings during the period of apostasy. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
What exactly is being debated? The effect of ridda and fisq on the qadi's office: whether the qadi automatically loses his position or remains until removed.
Two views exist within the Hanafi school.
2
State the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view: apostasy does not dismiss the qadi. He remains in office, but his judgments during apostasy are void.
This is from al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyya.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Apostasy is likened to fisq, and fisq alone does not cause dismissal. Also, appointment of a non-Muslim is valid in one narration, showing that disbelief does not inherently prevent the post.
The apostate's matter is suspended (mawquf), not dissolved.
4
Reconcile with the alternative view
The view that loss of religion causes dismissal can be reconciled by saying apostasy removes effectiveness (nufadh) of judgments, not the office itself.
Thus, the qadi's status remains, but his rulings are invalid.
5
Note the practical consequence
If the qadi repents, he continues in his post without need for reappointment. Also, a disbeliever can be validly appointed as qadi, though his rulings on Muslims are not binding.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الولوالجية : إذا ارتد القاضي أو فسق ثم صلح فهو على حاله لأن المرتد أمره موقوف ولأن الارتداد فسق وبنفس الفسق لا ينعزل إلا أن ما قضى في حالة الردة باطل بخلاف الحكم إذا ارتد فإنه يخرج والفرق مذكور فيها، وما قدمناه أنه ينعزل بفوات الدين يخالفه إلا أن يقال بالردة ينعزل عن نفاذ قضائه جمعاً البزازية من بينهما. وفي الواقعات الحسامية : الفتوى على أنه لا ينعزل بالردة فإن الكفر لا ينافي ابتداء القضاء في إحدى الروايتين حتى لو قلد الكافر ثم أسلم هل يحتاج إلى تقليد آخرء فيه روايتان ا هـ. وبه علمت أن ما في الخلاصة على خلاف المفتى به وعلمت أن تقليد الكافر صحيح وإن لم يصح قضاؤه على المسلم حال كفره.
And in al-Wilwaljiyya: If the qadi apostatizes or commits fisq and then reforms, he remains as he was because the apostate's matter is suspended, and because apostasy is fisq, and by fisq itself he is not dismissed, except that what he judged during apostasy is void, contrary to the case if a witness apostatizes, for he leaves (is disqualified), and the difference is mentioned in it. And what we previously said that he is dismissed by loss of religion contradicts this, unless it is said that by apostasy he is dismissed from the effectiveness of his judgment, as a compromise between the two. In al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyya: The fatwa is that he is not dismissed by apostasy because disbelief does not prevent the initial appointment of a qadi in one of the two narrations; even if he appoints a disbeliever and then he embraces Islam, is a new appointment needed? There are two narrations. Hence, you know that what is in al-Khulasa is contrary to the fatwa, and you know that the appointment of a disbeliever is valid, even though his judgment over a Muslim during his disbelief is not valid.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage addresses the controversial issue of whether apostasy (ridda) and fisq (sinfulness) automatically dismiss a qadi. The source al-Wilwaljiyya states that if a qadi apostatizes or commits fisq and then repents, he remains in office. The reasoning is that the apostate's matter is suspended (mawquf), and apostasy is a type of fisq, and fisq alone does not cause dismissal. However, judgments rendered during apostasy are void. This differs from a witness: if a witness apostatizes, his testimony is rejected. The author notes a contradiction with a previous statement that loss of religion (fawat al-din) causes dismissal. He suggests a reconciliation: apostasy may suspend the effectiveness of rulings but not the status of being qadi. Then the text cites al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyya, stating that the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is that apostasy does not dismiss because disbelief is not a barrier to initial appointment in one narration. Thus, appointing a disbeliever is valid initially, but his judgments over Muslims during his disbelief are invalid. This shows that the view in al-Khulasa (which says loss of religion causes dismissal) is not the operative position. Therefore, the appointment of a disbeliever as qadi is valid, though his rulings on Muslims are not binding while he remains a non-Muslim.

Key Terms
ردة (ridda): Apostasy from Islam.
فسق (fisq): Sinfulness, transgression.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; the apostate's legal status is held in abeyance until repentance or death.
نفاذ (nufadh): Effectiveness, enforceability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what happens to a qadi who apostatizes?
A) He is immediately dismissed
B) He remains in office but his judgments during apostasy are void
C) He must be reappointed after repentance
D) His appointment is invalid from the start

Key Takeaway
Apostasy does not automatically dismiss a qadi in the Hanafi school; it only voids his judgments during that period.

The Qadi's Qualifications Mirror the Witness's
Definition of qadi's basic qualifications via analogy with witness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the basic qualifications of a qadi as free, Muslim, adult, sane, and just, and explain why a disbeliever or child cannot be appointed.

Core Idea
A qadi's basic qualifications are identical to a witness's: free, Muslim, adult, sane, just.

The commentator starts by clarifying the matn's phrase 'its people are people of testimony.' This does not mean judgment is built on testimony, but rather that the same attributes qualify a person for both roles. This lesson unpacks those five essential conditions and the immediate consequence: a kafir or sabiyy cannot be a qadi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
The phrase 'أهله أهل الشهادة' is the starting point. Understand that it means the qadi and witness share the same basic attributes, not that judgment is built on testimony.
2
List the five core conditions
From the text: حر, مسلم, بالغ, عاقل, عدل. These are the fundamental qualifications derived from the analogy.
The condition 'just' (adala) is further discussed later.
3
Apply to appointment validity
Because a kafir lacks Islam and a child lacks puberty, their appointment as qadi is invalid. The text cites al-Bazzaziyya: a child who matures after appointment does not become valid.
4
Examine the exception
There is a narration from al-Waqi'at and al-Ajnas that if a kafir is appointed and then embraces Islam, he remains qadi. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that appointment of a kafir is invalid.
This shows an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : : أهله أهل الشهادة أي أهل القضاء أي من يصح منه أو من تصح توليته له لأن كلاً منهما يثبت الولاية على الغير والشاهد يلزم الحاكم أن بحكم بشهادته والحاكم والخصم بحكمه فكانا من باب واحد وليس المراد أن القضاء مبني على الشهادة ليلزم منه بناء القوي على الضعيف، وإنما المراد أنهما يرجعان في شيء واحد وهو أن يكون حراً مسلماً بالغاً عاقلاً عدلاً لا أن حكمه مبني على حكمها لكن أوصاف الشهادة أشهر عند الناس فعرف أوصافه بأوصافها وتمامه في النهاية فلا تصح تولية كافر وصبي فلذا قال في البزازية : قلد القضاء لصبي ثم أدرك لا يقضى به ؛ ذكره في المنتقى . وفي الأجناس : قلد القضاء الكافر ثم أسلم فهو على قضائه ولا يحتاج إلى تحديد ثان ا هـ . وفيها قبله : السلطان أمر عبده بنصب القاضي في بلدة ونصب يصح بطريق النيابة عن السلطان ولو حكم بنفسه لا يصح، ولو جمع بنفسه بعد أمره أو أمر غيره صح. الإمام أذن لعبده بالقضاء فقضى بعد ما عتق جاز ولا يحتاج إلى تجديد الإذن كما لو تحمل الشهادة في الرق ثم عتق ا.هـ. وقدمنا أن شرائط القاضي ثمانية وفي منظومة ابن وهبان وتولية الأطروش الأصح جوازها. وفسرها الشارح بأن يسمع ما قوي من الأصوات والأصم بخلافه وهو من لا يسمع ألبته. وفي القاموس : قوم طرش والأطرش الأصم. وظاهر كلامهم أن . من لا تقبل شهادته لم يصح قضاؤه ولا يرد الفاسق فإنه عندنا أهل لهما لأن القاضي لو قضى بشهادته صح وإن كان يأثم كما سيأتي، فعلى هذا لا يصح قضاء العدو على عدوه عدواة دنيوية كالشهادة وإن قلنا بصحته إذا قضى عزوه إلى البزازية لا إلى الخلاصة . قوله فلا تصح تولية كافر وصبي مخالف لما مر عن الواقعات . قوله : (قلد القضاء الكافر ثم أسلم على قضائه) هو إحدى الروايتين كما مر . قوله: (وقدمنا أن شرائط القاضي ثمانية) الذي قدمه تسعة وقد نظمها السيد الحموي فقال :
شروط القضاء تسع عليك بحفظها لتحرز سبقاً في طلابك للعلا بلوغ وإسـلام وعـقـل ومـنـطـق فصیح به فصل الخصومة قد حلا
توليه حكماً دون سـمـع لـدعـوة
وحرية سمع
والإبصار قد تلا
وفقدان حد القذف قد شرطوا له كما قال زين الدين في البحر مجملا
قوله : (وفي القاموس قوم طرش (الخ) قال الرملي وذكر في القاموس قبل قوله «قوم طرش»:
الطرش أهون الصمم وذكر في صمم الصمم محركه انسداد الأذنين وثقل السمع. قوله: (وظاهر كلامهم أن من لا تقبل شهادته لم يصح قضاؤه) هو عكس الكلية المذكورة في المتن. وقال في النهر :
His saying: 'Its people are people of testimony' means people of judiciary, i.e., one from whom it (judgeship) is valid or whose appointment is valid, because each of them establishes authority over others. The witness obliges the ruler to judge by his testimony, and the ruler and the opponent by his judgment, so they are of the same category. It does not mean that judgment is based on testimony to necessitate building the strong on the weak; rather, it means they share one thing: that he be free, Muslim, adult, sane, just, not that his judgment is based on its (testimony's) judgment. But the descriptions of testimony are more famous among people, so he defined its descriptions by its descriptions. The full explanation is in al-Nihaya. So the appointment of a disbeliever or a child is not valid. Hence, al-Bazzaziyya says: If he appointed a child as judge and then he matures, he does not judge by that (appointment). Mentioned in al-Muntaqa. And in al-Ajnas: If he appointed a disbeliever as judge and then he embraces Islam, he remains on his judgeship and does not need a new appointment. And before it in it: The ruler ordered his slave to appoint a judge in a town, and the appointment is valid by way of deputyship from the ruler. If he himself judges, it is not valid. And if he himself collects after his order or orders another, it is valid. The imam permitted his slave to judge; if he judged after being freed, it is permissible and does not require renewal of permission, just as if he bore testimony in slavery and then was freed. And we have previously mentioned that the conditions of a judge are eight. And in the poem of Ibn Wahban: 'And the appointment of the somewhat deaf (atrush) – the correct view is that it is permissible.' The commentator interpreted it as one who hears loud voices, unlike the completely deaf (asamm). In al-Qamus: 'qawm turush' and 'al-atrash' is the deaf. The apparent meaning of their words is that one whose testimony is not accepted, his judgment is not valid. But the fasiq is not excluded, because according to us he is qualified for both (judging and witnessing), because if a qadi judges by his testimony, it is valid, even if he sins, as will come. Therefore, the judgment of an enemy against his enemy due to worldly enmity is not valid, like testimony, even if we say it is valid if he judges – this is attributed to al-Bazzaziyya, not al-Khulasa. His saying: 'Thus the appointment of a disbeliever and a child is not valid' contradicts what came from al-Waqi'at. His saying: 'He appointed a disbeliever as judge and then he embraced Islam, he remains on his judgeship' is one of the two narrations, as came. His saying: 'And we have previously mentioned that the conditions of a judge are eight' – what he presented is nine, and al-Sayyid al-Hamawi has organized them in a poem: 'The conditions of judgeship are nine: upon you to memorize them to gain precedence in seeking excellence: puberty, Islam, intellect, clear speech to decide disputes, ability to judge without hearing, freedom, hearing, sight, and the absence of the hadd of qadhf – they have stipulated, as Zayn al-Din said in al-Bahr concisely.' His saying: (And in al-Qamus, 'qawm turush'…) al-Ramli said, 'al-turush is the lesser deafness, and it is mentioned under 'samm': al-samm is blockage of the ears and hardness of hearing.' His saying: 'And the apparent meaning of their speech that one whose testimony is not accepted, his judgment is not valid' is the reverse of the totality mentioned in the text. And he said in al-Nahr:
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's phrase 'أهله أهل الشهادة' (its people are people of testimony). The commentator explains that this means the qadi's qualifications are the same as those of a witness: free, Muslim, adult, sane, just. This is not because judgment is built on testimony, but because both share the same basic attributes. Therefore, a disbeliever or child cannot be validly appointed as qadi. However, as seen earlier, there is a narration that a disbeliever's appointment is valid, and if he converts, he remains qadi. The text then mentions the ruling about a slave: if the ruler orders his slave to appoint a judge, that appointment is valid as a deputyship; if the slave himself judges, it is not valid. A freed slave can judge without new permission, analogous to bearing witness after freedom. Then the passage discusses the conditions of a qadi. While the matn says there are eight conditions, the commentator notes that a poem lists nine: puberty, Islam, intellect, clear speech, ability to judge, freedom, hearing, sight, and absence of hadd qadhf. The passage also clarifies that a fasiq (sinner) is qualified to be a qadi (though he sins), but an enemy cannot judge his enemy, similar to testimony. Finally, it addresses the definition of deafness: 'atrash' is partial deafness (hears loud sounds), while 'asamm' is complete deafness. The apparent ruling that one whose testimony is rejected cannot judge is criticized as the reverse of the general principle.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahada): Testimony in court; used as a benchmark for qadi's qualifications.
عدالة (adala): Justice, integrity of character.
طرش (turush): Partial deafness; the atrush hears loud sounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for a qadi according to the poem cited in the text?
A) Puberty
B) Wealth
C) Islam
D) Freedom

Key Takeaway
A qadi must be free, Muslim, adult, sane, and just; a disbeliever or child cannot be validly appointed.

Qada — Judiciary

The Nine Conditions and Their Exceptions
Expansion of conditions with detailed exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list all nine conditions of a qadi as given in the poem, and discuss the exceptions for a fasiq, an enemy, and a partially deaf person.

Core Idea
A qadi must possess nine attributes: puberty, Islam, intellect, clear speech, ability to judge, freedom, hearing, sight, and absence of hadd qadhf.

While the matn mentions eight conditions, the commentator provides a poem listing nine. This lesson examines each condition, especially hearing and sight, and addresses special cases: a fasiq is still qualified (though sinful), an enemy cannot judge his foe, and an atrash (partially deaf) can be a qadi but not a fully deaf person.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the nine conditions from the poem
The poem by al-Sayyid al-Hamawi lists: بلوغ (puberty), إسلام (Islam), عقل (intellect), منطق (clear speech), توليه حكماً (ability to judge), حرية (freedom), سمع (hearing), إبصار (sight), وفقدان حد القذف (absence of hadd qadhf).
The matn says eight; the poem includes 'ability to judge' separately.
2
Clarify hearing and sight
The text distinguishes atrash (hears loud sounds) from asamm (completely deaf). The atrash can be a qadi; the asamm cannot.
Similarly, a blind person cannot be a qadi because sight is required.
3
Analyze the fasiq's eligibility
A fasiq (sinner) is considered 'adil for the role of qadi; his testimony is accepted and his judgments are valid, though he commits a sin by judging while being a fasiq.
This is a unique Hanafi position; other schools may require strict adala.
4
Consider the enemy judge
An enemy cannot judge his enemy in a case of worldly enmity, analogous to the rule for witnesses: an enemy's testimony is not accepted in favor of his enemy.
If the enmity is religious (e.g., a Muslim against a non-Muslim), it may be treated differently.
5
Examine the critique
The statement 'whoever's testimony is rejected, their judgment is invalid' is flawed. The fasiq is a counterexample: his testimony is accepted though he is a sinner, and his judgment is valid.
This shows that the relationship between witness and qadi qualifications is not fully symmetrical.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ruling on Appointing a Sinful Person (Fasiq) as Judge
Explaining eligibility and undesirability of appointing a fasiq as judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on appointing a fasiq as judge, explain the proof from Quran 49:6, and distinguish between legal eligibility and the undesirability of appointment.

Core Idea
A fasiq is eligible for judgeship, but his appointment is makruh tahrimi (close to forbidden).

This lesson from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a subtle but important distinction in Hanafi fiqh: a sinful person (fasiq) is technically eligible to serve as a judge, yet it is sinful to appoint him. The passage grounds this ruling in the Quranic command to verify news from a fasiq and analogizes it to testimony. It also presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement from Abu Yusuf regarding exceptional cases. By the end, you will be able to apply this ruling and understand its proof structure.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish eligibility from appointment
Identify that the passage first affirms eligibility (ahl) for judgeship, but then states it is not befitting (la yanbaghi) to appoint (yuqallad) a fasiq. Recognize the separation between capacity and actual designation.
Eligibility does not imply desirability.
2
Identify the primary proof (dalil)
Locate the Quranic verse (49:6) cited as evidence: 'If a fasiq comes to you with news, verify...' Understand how this verse is used by analogy: if a fasiq's testimony requires verification, his judgment likewise cannot be trusted, so appointing him is improper.
The proof is dalil naqli (Quran) with qiyas to testimony.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Note Abu Yusuf's exception: if the fasiq has high status (jah) and social propriety (muru'a), his testimony and judgeship are acceptable. This contrasts with the majority view that appointment is still makruh.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf vs. the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on the ruling for high-standing fasiqs.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The text indicates that the fatwa is upon the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding questioning witnesses in general, implying the majority position that appointment is makruh tahrimi. Abu Yusuf's exception is mentioned but not relied upon.
The majority position is the basis for fatwa.
5
Define key terms: fasiq and 'adala
Extract the definitions: fasiq is one who commits a major sin or persists on a minor sin; 'adala requires avoiding such sins and maintaining muru'a. Note that muru'a affects uprightness but may not make one fasiq.
Clarifies who is considered fasiq for this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والفاسق أهل للقضاء كما هو أهل للشهادة إلا أنه لا ينبغي أن يقلد) لما قدمنا أنهما من باب واحد، ولا ينبغي تقليده لأن القضاء من باب الأمانة والفاسق لا يؤتمن في أمر الدين لقلة مبالاته به كما لا ينبغي قبول شهادته فإن قبلها نفذ الحكم بها وفي غير موضع ذكر الأولوية عين الأولى أن لا تقبل شهادته وإن قبل جاز. وفي فتح القدير : ومقتضى الدليل أن لا يحل أن يقضى بها فإن قضى جاز ونفذ ا هـ . ومقتضاه الإثم وعلى الأول لا يأثم، وظاهر الآية يفيد أنه لا يحل قبولها قبل تعرف حاله وهي قوله إن جاءكم فاسق فتبينوا أن تصيبوا قوماً بجهالة فتصبحوا على ما فعلتم نادمين [الحجرات : ٦] وقولهم بوجوب السؤال عن الشاهد سراً وعلانية طعن الخصم أولاً في سائر الحقوق على قولهما المفتى به يقتضي أن يأثم بتركه لأنه للتعرف عن لا يقبل الفاسق. وصرح في اصلاح الإيضاح بأن . حتى يأثم وإن قبل القاضي شهادته يأثم واستثنى أبو يوسف من الفاسق إذا شهد أن يكون ذا جاه ومروءة فإنه يجب قبول شهادته كما في البزازية. فعلى هذا يجوز تقليده القضاء إلا أن يكون أبو يوسف فارقاً بينهما. والفسق لغة الخروج عن الاستقامة؛ كذا في المغرب. وشرعاً ارتكاب كبيرة أو الإصرار على صغيرة كما في الخزانة والعدالة اجتناب الكبائر والإصرار على صغيرة واجتناب فعل ما يخل بالمروءة كما سيأتي في الشهادات، فإذا ارتكب ما يخلها خرج عن كونه عدلاً وإن لم يصر فاسقاً . به حاله . من قلد فاسقاً
His saying: 'And the sinful person is eligible for judgeship just as he is eligible for testimony, except that he should not be appointed.' (Because we have mentioned that they are of one category, and he should not be appointed because judgeship is from the category of trust, and the sinful person is not trusted in religious affairs due to his lack of care, just as his testimony should not be accepted. But if it is accepted, the judgment based on it is effective. And in other places, it is mentioned as preferable: it is better not to accept his testimony, but if it is accepted, it is permissible. In Fath al-Qadir: 'The implication of the evidence is that it is not permissible to judge by it. But if he judges, it is permissible and effective.' Its implication is sin, and according to the first view, he does not sin. The apparent meaning of the verse indicates that it is not permissible to accept it before ascertaining his state, and it is His saying: 'If a sinful person comes to you, verify, lest you harm a people out of ignorance and become regretful over what you have done' (49:6). And their saying about the obligation of questioning the witness secretly and openly, and the opponent's objection first, in all rights according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the fatwa is upon it, implies that he sins by leaving it because it is for ascertaining that the sinful person is not accepted. In Islah al-Idah, it is clearly stated that even if the judge accepts his testimony, he sins. And Abu Yusuf made an exception among the sinful: if the witness is of high status and chivalry (muru'a), then his testimony must be accepted, as in al-Bazzaziyya. According to this, it is permissible to appoint him as judge, unless Abu Yusuf made a distinction between them. And fisq linguistically is exiting from uprightness; as in al-Mughrib. And technically, it is committing a major sin or persisting on a minor sin, as in al-Khazana. And 'adala (uprightness) is avoiding major sins and persistence on minor sins and avoiding actions that undermine muru'a, as will come in the chapter of testimonies. So if one commits something that undermines it, he exits from being upright even if he does not become a fasiq. His state... from appointing a fasiq.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on appointing a sinful person (fasiq) as a judge. The commentator first affirms that a fasiq is legally eligible for judgeship just as he is eligible for testimony, because both are based on the same principle of reliability. However, it is not befitting to appoint a fasiq because judgeship is a trust (amanah), and a fasiq lacks religious conscientiousness. The proof is the Quranic verse 49:6, which commands verification of news brought by a fasiq. The commentator discusses different opinions: some say it is merely disliked (makruh), while others say it is forbidden (haram) and sinful. Abu Yusuf made an exception for a fasiq of high social standing and chivalry, stating that his testimony must be accepted, and thus his judgeship is permissible. The passage then defines fisq and 'adala according to Hanafi terminology, noting that fisq is committing a major sin or persisting on a minor sin, while 'adala requires avoiding such and maintaining muru'a (social propriety).

Key Terms
فاسق (fāsiq): Linguistically: one who exits from uprightness. Technically: one who commits a major sin or persists on a minor sin.
عدالة (ʿadālah): Uprightness: avoiding major sins, persistence on minor sins, and actions that undermine muru'a.
مروءة (murūʾah): Social propriety or chivalry; avoiding actions that lower one's dignity in society.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on appointing a fasiq (sinful person) as a judge according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is absolutely forbidden and the judgment is invalid.
B) It is makruh tahrimi (close to forbidden) but if appointed, his judgments are valid.
C) It is permissible and recommended.
D) It is valid only if the fasiq repents.

Key Takeaway
A fasiq's technical eligibility for judgeship does not make his appointment permissible; the act of appointing is makruh tahrimi, but any judgments he issues are valid.

The Just Judge Who Becomes Sinful: No Automatic Dismissal
Explaining that a judge is not automatically dismissed by sin but deserves dismissal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a just judge who becomes sinful, explain the qiyas that supports it, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether his judgments are valid.

Core Idea
A judge's office is not voided by sin; appointment depends on initial qualification, not ongoing virtue.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a critical question in Islamic judiciary: what happens when a judge appointed while just later becomes sinful? The Hanafi school's answer is nuanced—the judge remains in office and his judgments are valid, but he deserves dismissal by the ruler. We will dissect the ruling, its proof via qiyas on an absconding slave, and the intra-school disagreement that exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that if a just judge becomes sinful (fasiq), he is not automatically dismissed (لا ينعزل) but deserves dismissal (يستحق العزل). His judgments remain valid except those based on bribery.
The ruling is specific to judges, not to the imam (ruler) who does not lose office by sin.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is a qiyas on the case of a slave authorized to trade who then absconds. The permission is not revoked even though the slave's behavior changed. Similarly, the judge's appointment was based on his condition at the time of appointment, not on a continuous condition of uprightness.
The qiyas is jaliyy (clear) and relies on the illa: appointment based on initial state.
3
Understand the illa
The effective cause (illa) is that the ruler's consent was given based on the judge's uprightness at the moment of appointment. Subsequent sin does not retroactively nullify that consent. This is analogous to the master's permission to the slave.
Some later scholars criticized this illa, but the apparent school adheres to it.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
In al-Nawadir, the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) held that the judgment of such a judge is not permissible (لا يجوز قضاؤه). This is a minority view within the school.
The passage also notes that if the ruler stipulates dismissal upon sin in the appointment contract, then the judge is dismissed automatically.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the judge does not automatically dismiss and his judgments are valid. This is the view of the majority of mashayikh and is considered the correct view in al-Khaniyya. The ruler should dismiss him, but his prior judgments stand.
The term 'deserves dismissal' (istiḥqāq al-ʿazl) is debated: some say it is wajib upon the ruler, others say it is mustahabb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو كان عدلاً ففسق لا ينعزل ويستحق العزل أي فسق بأخذ الرشوة أو بغيره من الزنا وشرب الخمر وما ذكره المؤلف من صحة تولية الفاسق وعدم عزله لو فسق هو ظاهر المذهب كما في الهداية وهو قول عامة المشايخ وهو الصحيح كما في الخانية. وعن علمائنا الثلاثة في النوادر أنه لا يجوز قضاؤه. وقال بعض المشايخ: إذا قلد الفاسق ابتداء . أن . وظاهر أن الكلية أعني من كان أهل الشهادة هو أهل للقضاء مطردة غير منعكسة عكساً لغوياً فلا يرد من فعل ما يخل بالمروءة فهو أهل للقضاء دون الشهادة ولا أن شهادة العدو على عدوه من حيث الدنيا لا تقبل وقضاؤه عليه صحيح. قوله : (كما قدمناه أقول : لم أره فيما مر نعم سيأتي بعد تسعة أوراق . قوله: (واستثنى أبو يوسف الخ) سيأتي في الشهادات عن الفتح أنه خلاف الأصح قوله : لو أبق المأذون ينحجر ولو أذن للآبق صح. وقيده في الخانية بما في يده عكس السائر على ألسنة الفقهاء وهو أسهل من الابتداء وإنما كان كذلك لوجود دليل يقتضيه وهو أن المقلد اعتمد عدالته فيتقيد التقليد بحال عدالته إلى آخر ما في النهاية. وفي البزازية : ولو شرط في التقليد أنه متى فسق ينعزل انعزل ا هـ . قيد بالقضاء لأن الفسق لا يمنع الإمامة بلا خلاف ولا ينعزل بالفسق وقوله «ويستحق العزل معناه يجب على السلطان عزله ؛ كذا في البزازية. وفي المعراج : يحسن عزله ا هـ. فقد اختلف في معنى الاستحقاق كما اختلف في توليته ابتداء.
His saying: 'And if he was upright and then becomes sinful, he is not dismissed and he deserves dismissal.' (i.e., becoming sinful by taking bribe or other sins like adultery and drinking wine. What the author mentioned regarding the validity of appointing a fasiq and his not being dismissed if he becomes sinful is the apparent school as in al-Hidayah, and it is the view of the general majority of mashayikh, and it is the correct view as in al-Khaniyya. From our three scholars in al-Nawadir: his judgment is not permissible. Some mashayikh said: If he appoints a fasiq initially... And it is apparent that the general principle, i.e., whoever is eligible for testimony is eligible for judgeship, is consistent but not reversible, so it is not invalidated by one who does something that undermines muru'a: he is eligible for judgeship but not for testimony, nor that the testimony of an enemy against his enemy in worldly matters is not accepted while his judgment against him is valid. His saying: 'As we have mentioned,' I say: I did not see it in what passed; indeed, it will come after nine pages. His saying: 'And Abu Yusuf made an exception' will come in testimonies from al-Fath that it is contrary to the most correct. His saying: If the authorized slave absconds, he is restricted; if he authorizes an absconder, it is valid. And he restricted it in al-Khaniyya to what is in his hand, contrary to the common saying of the jurists that it is easier than the beginning. This is so because there is a proof: the one who appointed relied on his uprightness, so the appointment is restricted to the state of his uprightness, etc. In al-Bazzaziyya: If he stipulated in the appointment that when he becomes sinful he is dismissed, then he is dismissed. He restricted it to judgeship because sin does not prevent imamate without disagreement, nor is he dismissed by sin. His saying 'he deserves dismissal' means it is obligatory upon the sultan to dismiss him; similarly in al-Bazzaziyya. In al-Mi'raj: it is good to dismiss him. So there is disagreement on the meaning of 'deserving' just as there is disagreement on appointing him initially.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage discusses the status of a judge who was just at the time of appointment but later becomes sinful (fasiq). The relied-upon Hanafi position is that the judge does not automatically lose his position; he remains a judge and his judgments are valid, except in the case of bribery. However, he deserves to be dismissed by the ruler. The passage notes that some early scholars (the three imams) in al-Nawadir held his judgments invalid. The author also discusses the analogy of an absconding slave with permission to trade: the permission remains even if the slave absconds. Similarly, the judge's appointment was based on his justice at the time, so it continues. If the ruler stipulated dismissal upon sin, then he is dismissed. The discussion also clarifies that this ruling applies only to judges, not to the imam (ruler), who does not lose his position by sin.

Key Terms
عزل (ʿazl): Dismissal from office.
استحقاق العزل (istiḥqāq al-ʿazl): Deserving dismissal; the judge is not automatically removed but the ruler is obliged to dismiss him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what happens to a just judge who later becomes fasiq?
A) He is automatically dismissed and all his judgments are invalid.
B) He is not dismissed, but his judgments are invalid.
C) He is not dismissed, his judgments are valid, but he deserves dismissal by the ruler.
D) He is dismissed only if he takes a bribe.

Key Takeaway
A judge does not lose office by sin; he must be formally dismissed by the ruler.

Establishing Sultanic Authority and the Effect of Sin
Ruling on methods of becoming sultan and effect of fisq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two methods of becoming sultan in the Hanafi school and determine whether a sinful sultan is dismissed based on his power.

Core Idea
Legitimacy comes from bay'ah or force; sin alone does not remove a powerful sultan.

How does a ruler become legitimate in the Hanafi view? And what happens if he becomes sinful? This lesson from Fatawa Qadikhan provides a pragmatic ruling: authority is recognized either by the pledge of the elite or by effective control, and a sinful ruler is not automatically deposed if he can enforce his rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two means of becoming sultan
Locate in the text the two methods: bay'ah (pledge of allegiance) of the nobles, and qahr wa ghalabah (domination and force). Note the condition for bay'ah: execution of judgment over the subjects.
The first method is contractual, the second is factual.
2
Understand the condition for bay'ah
The text states that mere bay'ah is insufficient; the candidate must actually enforce his judgments. If he cannot, he does not become sultan even if pledged.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on effective authority, not mere ceremony.
3
Analyze the case of the sinful sultan
If a sultan becomes fasiq (sinful) but has power (qahr), he is not dismissed. The reason given: dismissal would be futile because he would simply regain power by force.
This is a practical ruling: preventing chaos outweighs removing a sinful ruler.
4
Apply the same reasoning to a governor
The text compares a governor to a judge: he deserves dismissal but is not automatically dismissed. The same logic applies: if he has power, he stays.
The principle extends to any ruler with effective control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى قاضيخان من الردة والسلطان يصير سلطاناً بأمرين : بالمبايعة معه يعتبر في المبايعة مبايعة أشرافهم وأعيانهم. الثاني أن ينفذ حكمه على رعيته خوفاً من قهره وجبروته، فإن بايع الناس ولم ينفذ فيهم حكمه لعجزه عن قهرهم لا يصير سلطاناً، فإذا صار سلطاناً بالمبايعة فجار إن كان له قهر وغلبة لا ينعزل لأنه لو انعزل يصير سلطاناً بالقهر والغلبة فلا يفيد، وإن لم يكن له قهر وغلبة ينعزل ا هـ . ومن أول الدعاوى والوالي إذا فسق فهو بمنزلة القاضي يستحق العزل ولا ينعزل ا هـ .
And in Fatawa Qadikhan: From the chapter of apostasy: The sultan becomes sultan in two ways: (1) by bay'ah with him, and in bay'ah consideration is given to the bay'ah of their nobles and notables. (2) that his judgment is executed over his subjects due to fear of his domination and force. If people pledge allegiance but his judgment is not executed because he is unable to overpower them, he does not become sultan. If he becomes sultan by bay'ah, then if he is unjust but has power and domination, he is not dismissed because if he were dismissed he would become sultan by force and domination, so it is of no benefit. If he does not have power and domination, he is dismissed. And from the beginning of lawsuits: If a governor becomes sinful, he is like a judge: he deserves dismissal but is not dismissed.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage from Fatawa Qadikhan discusses the legitimacy of a sultan's authority. There are two ways: (1) by the pledge of allegiance (bay'ah) of the elite, and (2) by overpowering the subjects through force such that his judgments are executed. If he gains power through bay'ah but cannot enforce his rule, he is not a legitimate sultan. Once he is sultan, if he becomes sinful (fasiq) but maintains power, he is not dismissed because dismissal would serve no purpose – he would simply rule by force. However, if he is weak, he is dismissed. The same applies to a governor: he deserves dismissal but is not automatically removed.

Key Terms
سلطان (sulṭān): The ruler or sovereign with authority over a region.
مبايعة (mubāyaʿah): Pledge of allegiance, a contract of loyalty between the ruler and the ruled.
قهر وغلبة (qahr wa ghalabah): Domination and force; effective control through power.
فسق (fisq): Sinfulness, disobedience to Allah's commands; here, the ruler's personal immorality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a sultan becomes fasiq but has qahr wa ghalabah, what is his status?
A) He is automatically dismissed.
B) He is not dismissed.
C) He is dismissed only if the people demand it.
D) He must repent to remain sultan.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes two ways to become sultan, and a sinful sultan with effective control is not dismissed to avoid greater disorder.

Invalidity of Judgeship Acquired by Bribery and Its Effects
Explaining that judgeship via bribery is invalid and the judge is not a judge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a judgeship obtained by bribery is invalid and distinguish the three intra-Hanafi opinions on the validity of the judge's judgments.

Core Idea
A judgeship obtained through bribery is invalid; the bribed judge's judgments are void at least in the bribe case.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical issue of judicial integrity: if a person secures a judgeship by paying a bribe, is he a valid judge? And if he issues a judgment, is it enforceable? The Hanafi school has a nuanced intra-madhhab disagreement on the second question. Understanding this ruling is essential for upholding the sanctity of the judiciary in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling on the appointment
The text states: 'If he takes the judgeship by bribe, he does not become a judge.' This means the appointment itself is invalid (batil). Ask: what is the legal basis for this invalidity?
The appointment is invalid because it was based on corruption.
2
Understand the talil (reasoning) for invalidity
The text explains that the appointing authority relied on the person's character (adalah), but bribery indicates dishonesty, thus the condition of uprightness is missing.
The bribe itself is haram and undermines the integrity required for a judge.
3
Distinguish the three intra-Hanafi opinions on executing judgments
Three views exist: (1) al-Bazdawi: his judgments in the bribe case are executed (he is still a judge despite the bribe). (2) al-Sarakhsi: judgments in the bribe case are not executed, but others are valid. (3) a third view: all his judgments are invalid. Identify the proofs for each.
The disagreement hinges on whether the bribe-related fisq (immorality) affects judicial authority.
4
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq leans toward al-Sarakhsi's view by arguing that the fisq from bribery is effective because it involves acting for personal gain, contrary to the divine purpose of judgeship.
The preferred view: the judgment in the bribery case is void, but judgments in other matters may stand.
5
Apply the ruling to a concrete scenario
Consider a person who pays a government official to appoint him as a judge. He then rules in favor of his bribe-giver. According to the preferred view, what is the status of his ruling?
The ruling is invalid and should not be enforced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإذا أخذ القضاء بالرشوة لا يصير قاضياً أي بمال دفعه لتوليته لم تصح توليته (لأن المقلد اعتمد عدالته الخ) تعليل لما في الإيضاح قوله : وقيده في الخانية بما في يده) فيه إيجاز غير مفهم قال في النهاية : وأما على رواية فتاوى قاضيخان إنما يصح إذن الآبق في التجارة إذا أذن له في التجارة مع ذلك الرجل الذي كان العبد في يده . قوله : (ولم يذكر المؤلف نفاد قضائه) قال في النهر : في قوله «لا ينعزل» إيماء إلى أن قضاءه نافذ فيما ارتشى فيه وهذا أحد أقوال ثلاثة، والثاني لا ينفذ فيه وينفذ فيما سواه واختاره السرخسي، والثالث لا ينفذ فيهما والأول اختاره البزودي واستحسنه في الفتح لأن حاصل أمر الرشوة فيما إذا قضى بحق إيجاب فسقه وقد فرض أنه لا يوجب العزل فولايته قائمة وقضاؤه بحق فلم لا ينفذ، وخصوص هذا الفسق غير مؤثر، وغاية ما وجه أنه إذا ارتشى عامل لنفسه أو ولده معنى والقضاء عمل الله تعالى .اهـ. وأنت خبير بأن كون هذا الفسق غير مؤثر ممنوع بل يؤثر بملاحظة كونه عملاً لنفسه، وبهذا يترجح ما اختاره خصوص
His saying: 'And if he takes the judgeship by bribe, he does not become a judge' (i.e., he paid money for his appointment, so his appointment is not valid). (Because the one who appointed relied on his uprightness, etc.) This is a clarification of what is in al-Idah. His saying: 'And he restricted it in al-Khaniyya to what is in his hand' contains an abbreviation that is not understood. Al-Nihaya said: According to the narration of Fatawa Qadikhan, the permission for the absconding slave in trade is valid only if he gives him permission to trade with the man in whose possession the slave is. His saying: 'And the author did not mention the execution of his judgment.' Al-Nahr said: In his saying 'he is not dismissed' there is an indication that his judgment is executed in the matter for which he took bribe. This is one of three opinions. The second: it is not executed in that matter but is executed in others, chosen by al-Sarakhsi. The third: it is not executed in either. The first was chosen by al-Bazdawi and considered preferable in al-Fath, because the result of the bribe is that he judged by right, and his fisq does not necessitate the invalidity of the judgment. And this specific fisq is not effective. And you are aware that the claim that this fisq is not effective is invalid; rather it is effective because it is an act for himself or for his child, and judgeship is the work of Allah. And by this, the chosen opinion is favored.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on someone who obtains judgeship by bribing. The relied-upon view is that such an appointment is invalid and he does not become a judge. However, there is a difference of opinion on whether his judgments are executed. Some say they are not executed because his fisq is related to bribery, which makes him act for himself. Others say they are executed because his fisq does not remove his authority. The analogy of the absconding slave is used to show that the appointment is conditioned on the judge's justice at the time of appointment, and bribery is a separate issue. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq leans toward the view that the fisq from bribery is effective in invalidating judgments in the bribe case, thus favoring al-Sarakhsi's position over al-Bazdawi's.

Key Terms
رشوة (rishwah): Bribe; payment to obtain an official position or favorable judgment.
تولية (tawliyah): Appointment to a position, especially judgeship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct view regarding the validity of judgments made by a judge who obtained his position through bribery?
A) All his judgments are valid because he is officially a judge.
B) His judgments in the bribe case are invalid, but others are valid.
C) All his judgments are invalid.
D) His judgments are valid if he is qualified.

Key Takeaway
The appointment of a judge via bribery is invalid; his judgment in the bribery case is void due to the effective fisq, but other judgments may remain valid according to the preferred Hanafi view.
